This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission which visited
Zaire in March 1988, and consisted of Messrs. Alain P. Ballereau (Senior
Economist and Task Manager), Jean Grosdidier de Matons (Institutional
Development Advisor), Agustin Litvak (Port Engineer), Colin Lyle (Financial
Analyst), Sujeer Nayak (Railway Engineer) and Benedict de St. Laurent
(Economist Consultant). Messrs. Cung Tran Luu (Human Resources Specialist)
and Carlos de Castro (Multimodal Transport Specialist) contributed to this
report. Mrs. Armelle Nau assisted in producing this report.

ZAIRE
First Transport Rehabilitation Pro1ect (ONATRAISNCZ)
Credit and Prolect Summary
Borrower:          Republic of Zaire
Beneficiaries:     Office National des Transports (ONATRA)
Societe Nationale des Chemins de Fer Zairois (SNCZ)
Atount:             SDR 57.3 million (US $75 million equivalent)
Terms:             Standard IDA Terms
Onlending:         20 years including 5 years9 grace period at 7.65%
interest. Foreign exchange risk tc be borne by ONATRA
and SNCZ.
Project Description: The project would incl'ie:
(i)   Key policy measures for:
- institutional reform at ONATRA, including
a) deacentralizing main departments
b) reducing loss-making and auxiliary activities
c) privatizing the shipyards and
d) establishing responsible management with financial controls
adapted to the new structure;
- financial recovery in line with plans proposed by ONATRA and SNCZ
and in the context of Contrat-Programmes with Government,
including
a) debt restructuring of ONATRA
b) reducing surplus personnel while training others
c) controlling consumables and inventories
d) tightening collection of receivables
e) using cost-based tariffs and
f) strengthening accounting procedures and performance monitoring
with a new MIS;
- operational improvement, including
a) organizing and performing adequate maintenance
b) setting operational targets and monitoring achievement
c) improving capacity utilization through better marketing of
return hauls and
d) rehabilitating works and renewing equipment to maintain and not
expand present capacities; and
(ii)  Investments will be aimed at rehabilitating the Voie Nationale (VN),
the backbone of the transport system consisting of an integrated
chain of rail, port and river facilities controlled by ONATRA and
SNCZ. For ONATRA, the project would include ports improvement and
equipment replacement (30%), barges, pushers and spares (23Z),



- vi -
ral3way workshops, tools and spares (17%), and containers, office
technology, technical assistance and training (30%). For SNCZ,
investments would include about 300 kms of track renewal (46%),
locomotive replacement (26%) and rolling stock rehabilitation (12%),
renewal of telecommunications and computer, TAs and training (16%).
Major track renewal work would be contracted out, and TAs teams with
agreed performance targets would share management responsibilities.
In addition, the project would help finance a program to encourage
voluntary early retirements in order to raise the productivity of
both over-staffed enterprises by providing incentives which could
include training for alternative outside employment, and the
provision of credits or grants for the purchase of capital goods for
use in such employment (4 percent). No part of the Credit would be
disbursed against any cash payments to employees or retirees.
Benefits: Economic benefits are unit operating costs reduced by about
102, transit time halved to 22 days between Shaba and Matadi and lower
inventories and more reliable stock supplies for copper production.
Improved performance on the VN should generate traffic from local trade,
farming exports, increasing its profitability and saving foreign exchange
on alternative routes. ERR overall is about 14% and ranges between 10 and
20%.
Risks. There are three main risks: (i) the extent to which managerial
improvements will take place; (ii) Government failure to abide by the
contrat-programne (e.g. not approving tariff adjustment or not
compensating the enterprises for social services) might jeopardize the
project financial objectives and force the two agencies to suspend most of
their social transport services; and (iii) the reopening of the Lobito
route through Angola. To minimize the first risk, a critical mass of
technical assistance has been included under the project to ensure that
operational performance will improve and a detailed training program has
been agreed to ensure that skilled Zairians are available. As for the
second risk, the contrat-programme with SNCZ has already been signed, and
that with ONATRA negotiated, and the first tariffs adjustments grant. d.
Close supervision will, however, be necessary. Finally, the third risk
has been carefully assessed. Were the situation to return to conditions
prevailing prior to the Lobito closing, economic returns on SNCZ track
rehabilitation would increase, since Lobito traffic would have to use a
large portion of the SNCZ track to be rehabilitated. This traffic
includes the Zairean traffic currently diverted to southern routes, part
of which would revert to the VN, as would some of the Zambian copper-
exports now transiting through the longer South African routes. On the
rest of the VN, the traffic pattern is likely to remain unchanged since
the Lobito closing did not affect it, and reopening is not expected to
affect it either. Overall, the economic return on SNCZ investments would
increase, and the VN would maintain its competitiveness.

I.  THE TRANSPORT SECTOR AND THE NVOIE RATIONALE
A. GeograDhic and Economic Settint
1.01     Zaire is the third largest country in Africa and the fifth largest
in population, but its GNP per capita (estimated at US$170 in 1986) ranks
among the lowest. Zaire has a total population of some 33 million and a
density of 12 persons per square kilometer. About a third of the
population is urban.  Agriculture (commercialized and subsistence) ac_ounts
for only 30 percent of GDP but it provides employment and income for more
than three-quarters of.the population. Mining and mineral processing
account for about one-third of GDP and two-thirds of export earnings.
Mining has traditionally been a major source of public revenue.
1.02     After relative stability and economic growth which lasted until
1974, when GDP grew at about seven percent per year in real terms, the
Zairean economy between 1975 and 1983 had a series of crises and short-
lived recoveries, characterized by severe urnder-utilization and
deterioration of productive capacity and infrastructure, the emergence of
significant economic and financial imbalances, high inflation, and a
decline in per capita income. Factors contributing to this were: the
zairianization measures of 1973-74 which, though subsequently rescinded,
destroyed the distribution network and confidence in the private sector;
the heavy external borrowing of the early 1970s, much of it at unfavorable
terms and for projects of low or even negative returns; continuing
deterioration in terms of trade; and deficiencies in economic management.
1.03     In 1983, tc improve the economic and financial situation,
Government 6.arted a stror.g stabilization program that was supported by the
Fund with large debt reschedulings. The convertibility of the currency was
restored in 1984 and a market-oriented determination of exchange rate was
adopted; nearly all imports restrictions Imp-rts were lifted and most
domestic prices were liberalized; fiscal discipline was strengthened;
external and internal imbalances were reduced and external debt service
payments (after rescheduling) were made punctually.
1.04     In 1986, structural Padjustment measures were taken by Government
in order to address the problems which had hampered economic growth. As
part of its industrial sector adjustment program supported by IDA and SFA
credits, it implemented tariff reforms and introduced investment
incentives. However, from 1986 onward a severe deterioration in terms of
trade exerted additional pressure on the economy. Reduced toreign exchange
receipts led to a decline in imports; adjustment measures that were taken
proved insufficient and the budget and the exchange rate came under
increased pressure. The resulting monetary expansion put further pressure
on domestic prices and on the exchange rate, and the zaire depreciated
rapidly.
1.05     In mid 1987, the Government agreed with the Fund and the Bank on
adjustment measures to cope with the persistent external constraints.
Under a new stand-by arrangement with the Fund and a Structural Adjustment
Credit (SAC) with the Bank, a program was set to (a) restore private sector
confidence; (b) create an incentive system to stimulate exports and
efficient-import substitution industries; (c) enable agricultural
production to respond to liberalization policies; (d) restructure public



- 2 -
expenditures to rehabilitate the country's infrostructure and provide
better basic public services; and (e) reform and rationalize public
enterprises. As part of the SAC, a number of measures were agreed to
improve ONATRA and SNCZ financial situation; financial recovery plans were
to be prepared ba-ed on cost-based tariffs and regularly adjusted against
inflation; institutional measures were to streamline their organizations
and staffing. This program had mixed results and the release of a second
tranche was postponed until revised satisfactory macro-economic objectives
could be agreed.
1.06     The  oncern to improve performance of public enterprises includes
in particular the transport sector which has suffered from inappropriate
past investments, overstaffing and weak management. With the exception of
Gecamines (the mining parastatal) the contribution of the public
enterprises to the Government's budget in the form of taxas and dividend
payments is negligible, while the Government implicitly subsidizes a number
of public enterprises by servicing their external debt.
B. The Transport System and Orftanization
1.07     Zaire's transport system is e network of rivers, railways and road
linked at strategic points. The dominant elements are the Zaire River and
its tributaries providing some 15,000 km of navigable waterways and about
5,000 km of railways, forming a raillriver system for inter-regiLnal
transport and external access. Most of the 145,000 km of roads, of which
only 2,400 km are paved, complement the rail and river network, although
some now compete in particular between Matadi and Kinshasa. Road transport
is becoming increasingly important in the distribution of basic foodstuffs,
both imported through Matadi and local. The country has one main seaport,
Matadi, and two major inlaud river ports, Kinshasa and Ilebo, which are
hubs for rail and river transport.  There are about 50 airfields and
airatrips in Zaire, of which about 15 are served by local airlines. Five
airfields can accommodate wide-bodied aircraft.  Two pipelines transport
refined petroleum products from Matadi to Kins%asa.
1.08     The development o.' Zaire's transport system has been influenced by
the river network, the distribution of the population and the location of
mineral deposits in the southeast. Overall, the transport system is barely
adequate for the country's development or for its potential resources.
There are wide regional disparities and the system's capacity is severely
limited by low equipment availability, poor infrastructure and considerable
operational inefficIenev due to institutional and managerial weaknesses.
Zaire's economic declint the shortage of local funds and foreign exchange,
inappropriate decisions   acerning tariffs, budget allocations, and
investment incentives have resulted in low levels of maintenance and asset
replacement.
1.09     Current modernization efforts under this and other projects need
to be accompanied by a restructuring and a rehabilitation of some of the
key transport agencies to improve their efficiency and reorient their
strategy. The proposed Transport Rehabilitation Project extends these
modernization efforts to two important transport agencies, the Office
National des Transports (ONATRA) and the Socikti Nationale des Chemils de
Fer Zairois (SNCZ). Detailed review is given in Chapters II and IV.



- 3 -
1.10     The other main transport parastatal is the Office des Routes (OR),
the agency responsible for national and provincial roads and beneficiary of
a proposed Second Transport Rehabilitation Project. Smaller parastatals
also playing an important role in transport are: (i) Regie des Voies
Maritimes (RVM) and Regie des Votes F'uviales (RVF), created in early 1972
with the Bank's assistance and responsible for maintainit.g the
infrastructure for water transport, (ii) Regie des Voies Aeriennes (RVA)
which builds, maintains and operates airports; (iii) Air Zaire, the state-
owned airline with scheduled domestic and international services; and (iv)
Compagnie Maritime Zairoise (CMZ), the national shipping company. Exuept
for OR, which is under the Ministry of Public Works (MPW), all are under
the technical control of the Ministry of Transport and Communications
(MTC); and the financial zontrol of the Ministry of Portfolio (MPF).  In
addition, the Ministry of Planning (MP) coordinates transport investments
with the needs of other sectors.
(a) Office des Routes
1.11     OR is responsible for construction and maintenance of about 57,000
km of roads.  Because of its strained resources, it has limited its
coverage to the 40,900 km of national and priority regional roads. It has
about 8,000 employees and management is operationally and financially
autonomous and geographically decentralized. One of OR's major probl.ms is
the age of its equipment, about one-half of which is being renewed as part
of its comprehensive 1986-88 highway program. Despite all the difficulties
encountered in the 1970's, OR has now matured into an organized and
professionally competent institution.  In 1985 for the first time, it
established a comprehensive program of activities and investments to be
carried out in 19d6-88 with financing from a number of external donors
including IDA through the Sixth Highway Project (1985). The execution of
this program has been delayed due to inadequate local funding; solutions
have been proposed in May 1988 but remain to be implemented (para. 1.35).
(b) Other Public Agencies
1.12     The three "Regies" (RVA, RVF, RVH) had difficulty in fulfilling
their mandates. RVF and RVM in partir-.lar had serious technical, financial
and managerial problems that    . ieing overcome, a process that IDA helped
to initiate and is support.Lng through the Navigation Improvement Project
(Cr. 1761-ZR) approved in 1987, cofinanced with Belgian bilateral
assistance and USAID. RVA suffers from deteriorating facilities and
insufficient resources. An assessment of its main needs is currently
underway with IDA's assistance. The overall performance of Air Zaire and
CMZ has been poor; both have lost much ground to international and local
competition and are in a weak financial position. In 1986 Government took
stringent measures to improve their situation, including major staff
reductions (two-thirds of Air Zaire staff in particular), sale of a DC-10,
retirement of three cargo ships, and the establishment of management
contracts with international companies.
(c)  Pr_vate Sector
1.13     There is very little regulation in the transport sector.  Although
parastatal agencies still predominate, the number of private operators is
increasing. A substantial part of their traffic is agricultural, or
industrial or commercial companies transporting on their own eccourt. Road



- 4 -
transport has traditionally been assured by the private sector and this
market is very competitive, (particularly around Kinshasa) which has helped
to keep tariffs low.   There are about 700 firms with ten or more trucks
each and an estimated 2,500 small operators with about one or two vehicles
employing about 27,000 people. In the hinterland, road transport has been
constrained by the lack of petroleum. The private river transport industry
is also very diversified and employs about 17,000 people. About 30 modern
companies with about 200 vessels between them provide the bulk of the
private capacity (representing about 20% of ONATRA's traffic). Only one of
them is a specialized transporter, and most of the freight is carried by
river transporters on their own account, with only 10 or 20% of the load
for outsiders. In addition to the large companies, there are about 650
small owners (mostly traders) with one or two boats, generally wooden
whalers with a capacity of 10 to 20 tons. There are about 17 private air
operators with some 9,500 employees, mostly concentrating on air freight,
and often under capitalized. The fleet has a capacity of about 400T, with
45 planes, of 13 different makes, mostly of obsolete technologies, and very
expensive to run.
C. Performance of the Transport Sector
1.14     Traffic in Zaire is characterized by:  (a) a fast expansion of
road transport close to large cities while rail and river transport is
stagnant; (b) a decreasing share carried by parastatal companies (43% in
1:j86 for freight transport); (c) the relatively high volume of air
transport; (d) a low traffic density (1.3 passenger x km and 0.6 ton x km a
day per capita in 1986) and (e) heavy local traffic, representing three
quarters of passenger transport and one-third of freight transport.
1.15     Transport volumes in the past 20 years have followed closely the
changes in the country's GNP. Traffic increased in the late 1960s and
early 19709, fell abruptly in the mid-1970s and has since been stagnant.
Today, traffic levels are about the same as in the late 1960s, i.e., about
25% below the mid-1970s levels and far below the peaks of the mid-1950s.
This trend has been aggravated by the closure of the Benguela railway in
Angola with the resulting loss of all Zambian transit traffic and exports
of Zairean manganese (production of which has been stopped). Mining
activities account for about one-half of :otal rail and one-third of river
traffic. The decline of the parastatals' share is a relatively recent
phenomenon but ONATRA and SNCZ still account for about 16% and 28%
respectively of total freigh't traffic. Table 1.01 (T)1 shows traffic
distzibution for 1986. ONATRA and SNCZ transport activity from 1976 to
1987 is shown in Table 1.01 and the distribution of freight transport
market in Table 1.02. Although light in volume, air transport is unusually
developed compared to most African countries, providing key links to and
from remote areas, particularly Kivu.

1.16     The Private Sector has developed rapidly, representing in 1975,
about 14% of river transport and 27% of domestic air tra.fic tonnage while
by 1986, its respective shares had risen to 34% and 76%. Similarly,
private road transport has developed substantially at the expense of the
railways of ONATRA and SNCZ whose local freight and passenger traffic have
decreased. Between Matadi and Kinshasa, trucking's share of imports has
increased from 15% in 1975 to 53% in 1986, while CFMK's share has decreased
(Table 1.03). Chart 1 illustrates the shares of traffic along the Voie
Nationale in 1986. The reliability and higher quality of service offered
by private operators are the main reasons for this increase. However, the
private sector does not provide public transport services that are left to
ONATRA and SNCZ to operate, mainly for long distance passengers and
smallholder agriculture products.
1.17     The quality of transport service is generally unsatisfactory.
Scarce available data indicate that differences in levels of service exist
between transport modes and operators, in particular in reliability/
security between parastatals (mainly ONATRA and SNCZ) and orivate
operators. Losses and damage are minimal for goods shipped by the latter



- 6 -
while they are reported to be substantial when shipped by ONATRA and SNCZ.
In addition, private operators systematically reimburse losses to clients,
wheteas the coverage and actual reimbursement are very unsatisfactory with
both parastatals. It will take time to reverse such a poor performance and
until then customers will concinue to favor private operators.
1.18     Transport costs are generally very high.  This is due  to the very
low traffic density of rail, river and road networks r'sulting from Zaire's
vast area, and difficulty to achieve scale economies. The 1986 average
cost per traffic unit (TU) 2 on ONATRA's railway is about US$0.07/TU and
about double on SNCZ, or approximately twice the cost on Kenya or Zambia
RL.ilways (Table 1.04). This is also due .o poor management, differences in
accounting (particularly concerning valuation of assets), high fuel costs,
old equipment, etc. Purther, ONATRA and SNCZ's equipment is not used
efficiently, losses (especially of fuel) are high, productivity per staff
is low and overhead costs are considerable. River transport costs are more
attractive, about US$0.05/TU for ONATRA but somewhat higher on average for
private operators. .ae cost of road transport varies greatly according to
road quality. Rough estimates tend to show that costs are average on paved
roads (about US$0.07/TU) but high on earth roads (up to US$0.20/TU)
compared to other African countries.
D. The Voie Nationale and Alternative International Routes
1.19     The main traffic corridor in Zaire is the Voie Nationale (VN), a
multimodal chain of transport consisting of the railway line from the Shaba
mining area to Ilebo port on the Kasai River, barge transport from Ilebo to
Kinshasa on the Kasai and Zaire rivers, rail transport from Kinshasa to
Matadi, and maritime transport from Matadi port through the Zaire estuary
to the sea. The VN is crucial to Zaire because it is the only evacuation
route for its mineral exports which is located entirely within the country.
Freight and passenger traffics are shown on Table 1.05 and 1.06
respectively. Container flows along the VN are presented in Chart 2.
1-20     The Voie Nationale is 2,655 kilometers long but is handicapped by
two intermediate rail/river trans-shipments points at Ilebo and Kinshasa.
It competes with three other rail routes for heavy traffic (Map 1):
(i)      the Dar Es Salaam route (2,623 kms) via tne Tanganyika Lake; it
has the same handicap of two trans-shipments at Kalemie and Kigoma
to cross the lake;
(ii)     the Dar Es Salaam route (2,414 kms) via the TAZARA (Tanzania-
Zambia Railway) is the shortest now that t'.e Benguela Railway
through Angola is closed. However, this line gives some priority
to Zambian traffic;
(iii)    two southern routes, a thousand kilometers longer than the VN
(3,522 kms to Durban and 3,623 kms to East London) but which are
continuous and efficiently operated, allowing the shortest transit
time overall (about 36 days vs. 50 for the VN and more than 50 for
the Dar Es Salaam routes). The 1986 average speed for copper
exports was 4.1 kms per hour on these routes vs. 2.2 kms per hour
on the VN.
2/ One TU being defined as one x Ton km or two passenger x km.



Zairean mineral traffic for each export route (1970-1987) is shown on Table
1.07 and the transit time (1972-1987) or. Table 1.08.
1.21     A road network, essentially used for Zaire's imports, complements
these alternative railway, river, or lake routes. A good paved road links
Shaba to South-Af-4can ports and production centers from where most
equipment and manufactured goods consumed by Gecamines are shipped. A
paved road serves Dar Es Salaam (Tanzam highway), but is used less as
economic relationships are weaker. A difficult track only serviced by
convoys during the dry season goes through Kasai to Kinshasa and then
Matadi. Transit time by road to Shaba is about 10 days from Durban, 7 to
10 days from Dar Es Salaam (via Zambia), and 15 to 20 days from Matadi.
1.22     Port terminals also play an important role in route definition.
The best port facilities are located in South Africa, where Durban and East
London provide good service with large storage areas. Dar Es Salaam is
often congested. Matadi is a relatively efficient port by African
standards, but is penalized by its difficult location 140 kilometers
upstream and its institutional set up (para. 2.05).
1.23     The main factors influencing the choice of route are:
(i)      National interest.  The Zairean Government asks the mining
companies to ship the maximum volumes of minerals by the VN to
avoid hard currency outlays. Over the last few years, the VN has
carried the highest proportion of mineral traffic (55% of total
copper exports in 1986 and in 1987, vs about 50% in the early
80s);
(ii)     Technical or operational constraints.  Even at current modest
traffic levels, some capacity limitations exist on the VN: transit
time increases each time the mining industry increases its
shipments, and ONATRA refuses to handle more than 5,200 tons a
month of copper blister (352 of total Gecamines production),
because it is difficult to handle, except in containers;
(iii)    Supply security.  As the reliability of the VN is low, it is less
and less used for imports. In 1987, Gecamines often came close to
shutting-down some factories for lack of imported supplies. As a
result, except for petroleum products, imports on the VN have
dropped from about 55,000 tons p.a. in the early 80s to less than
30,000 now;
(iv)     Customer requirements.  Although Dar Es Salaam port has an
important transit delay and TAZARA gives priority to Zambian
traffic, some Chinese or Indian copper buyers still want delivery
in Dar Es Salaam;
(v)      Financial management.  The mining industry chooses its shipment
route to optimize its inventory or/and treasury situation, taking
into account changes in copper price and foreign exchange needs
which generally favor the shortest transit time.
1.24     Nevertheless, the VN's share of Zaire's mineral exports since
1970, has ranged between 42 and 55% (Table 1.07). Despite the difficulties
of SNCZ and the poor condition of ONATRA's equipment and facilities, the VN



is presently carrying more copper than since independence. The closure of
the Benguela Railway in 1975 did not affect the VN traffic as Zairean
mineral export that used this route (about 90 to 190,000 tons) switched to
the southern routes. Since then, traffic shares on alternative routes have
remained unchanged. Import volumes on the VN have varied in recent years
from about 75,000 to 100,000 tons for general cargo and from 120,000 to
140,000 tons for petroleum products mostly destined for Shaba, particularly
as it has become more advantageous to buy petroleum on the international
market and ship it from Matadi than from Zambia.
1.25     Transit time for copper exports on the VN has deteriorated from 20
days in the early 1970s to 51 days in 1983 and 53 days in 1986, about 30%
more than on the southern route but has lowered to an average of 46 days '.n
1987 due to improved management (Table 1.08). These delays are aggravated
by the high cost of financing the stock of minerals in transit.3   The
journey is particularly long, due to poor planning, insufficient stocks at
appropriate time of the year at Ilebo, high derailment frequency on SNCZ's
tracka, poorly organized river transport, slow handling in the ports of
Kinshasa and Ilebo, long train detention at yards, and poor operating
speeds on SNCZ's network. Domestic transit time for containerized imports,
averaging about 55 days, is also unsatisfactory.
1.26     The southern routes and TAZARA offer the most competition to the
VN; firstly they are all rail routes and no trans-shipment is required;
secondly they are already used for much of Shaba's imports so the return
transport of minerals is economical; in addition, they are efficiently
operated and about 10% to 15% cheaper than the VN (although requiring
payment in foreign currency). Map 2 shows a comparison of tariffs by
routes between Shaba and Europe. On the other hand, the VN provides
employment, allows Zaire better control of its export-transport, fosters
national unity and is a necessary insurance policy against political risks
and disturbances in the southern region. The VN whether it is controlled
by two large state companies as ONATRA and SNCZ must, to stay open, become
more efficient to be able to compete better with the other routes in terms
of cost and rapidity.
1.27     At present, Government transport development strategy (which is
fully supported by IDA) is to maintain the role of these two parastatals in
operating the VN because it cannot be taken over by the domestic private
sector. This is due to the latter limited capacity to (i) compete
effectively on such long distances, given the lack of an acceptable road
alternative; and (ii) acquire the very large sunken investments of ONATRA
and SNCZ. Further, it would be unwise to attempt to substitute rail and
river transport by road, since development of the road network has been
very slow and its maintenance inadequate due to severe budget shortage.
Also, road transport could not be competitive within Zaire during the rainy
season as the road network even improved would not be an all-weather
network as, because of low traffic, it would have to remain unsurfaced.
The parastatals role however should be limited to transport activities that
can be organized efficiently while other local transport and non-transport
activities should be passed on to the private sector (paras. 2.31 and 3.13
to 3.22).
3/ At a 12% discount rate, Zaire could save about $4.0 million annually if
transit time of mineral exports was reduced by one month.



1.28     The proposed project aims specifically at improving the
reliability and speed of service on the VN, through limited rehabilitation
and operational improvements on ONATRA and SNCZ networks (Chapter V). With
reduction of idling time of trains at stations and barges at ports and with
the drastic simplification in port operations resulting from container use,
much progress can be achieved especially when the ongoing improvements to
the Kinshasa port facilities are completed. The project would help finance
in particular a major track renewal and motive power replacement program
for SNCZ without which it would not be possible for the VN to stay open and
remain Zaire's ma:  development corridor.  With the reduction in operating
costs, transport tariffs on the VN should become competitive with the
southern routes. In addition, with the savings in minerals export
inventory expected from the halving of transit time, the VN should become
the cheapest and most competitive alternative route (para. 6.10).
E.  Main Sector Issues and  Objectives
1.29     The major problems of the sector, as identified in the
Government's Five Year Development Plan (1986-1990) are: (i) the physical
deterioration of both plant and equipment, particularly along the VN; (ii)
the lack of overall coordination and planning in public sector transport
iavestments; and (iii) poor management and low efficiency of state
companies. These problems, while being addressed under ongoing projects,
have not yet been satisfactorily resolved. In some cases, economic
constraints have rendered the financial and operational situation of
ONATRA, SNCZ and other companies more precarious than before. As a
consequence, and in agreement with Government the following main objectives
have been singled out in the transport sector under the recently approved
SAC (para. 1.05) and the first four will be addressed under this proposed
Transport Rehabilitation Project:
(i)      Strengthening the financial situation of ONATRA and SNCZ through
improved financial management together with cost savings measures,
substantial tariff increases and revision of their structures;
(ii)     Implementing a reorganization of ONATRA, based on
decentralization, streamdin!ng and divesting (privatization) some
activities;
(iii)    Reducing transport operating costs mainly on the VN by repairing
and replacing facilities and equipment and strengthening operating
methods (cargo handling, containerization, controls...);
(iv)     Ensuring consistency between transport policy and country
development policy;
(v)      Improving transport planning, programming and budgeting;
(vi)     Ensuring adequate local funding of Office des Routes to carry out
its maintenance program and rehabilitate high priority roads;
(vii)    Launching a rural roads improvement program and establishing a
decentralized implementation system controlled by local
administration; and
(viii)   Strengthening RVA's role and improving airport infrastructure.



- 10 -
Improving Performance of ONATRA and SNCZ
1.30     In the short term, the operational and financial situation of both
ONATRA and SNCZ requires urgent action to stem the rapid deterioration of
assets and operations. Delays in granting tariff increases in a highly
inflationary environment are at the root of the problem. Appropriate
tariff structures to replace the existing ad valorem tarification should b.
developed in parallel with cost reductions and efficiency improvement
measures. Those will be implemented as part of financial recovery plans
that have been prepared by the agencies (Chapters II and IV).
1.31     Beyond immediate remedies, there is a need to attack the deep
seated structural problems such as decreasing or stagnating traffic, their
high administrative and social overhead costs and lack of manaAerial and
technical capabilities. This will involve a major institutional reform
firstly at ONATRA and later at SNCZ. As both entities will continue to
play a crucial role in the Zairean economy and will require continued
support from the donor community, their mandate and objectives need to be
clarified, in relation to the compeLitive and dynamic private sector. The
Government intends to redefine their roles and responsibilities and to
streamline their organization accordingly (Chapter III).
1.32     The above financial and institutional program will need to be
complemented by a limited investment program aimed principally at (a)
eliminating current bottlenecks, (b) improving the efficiency of
operations, particularly with respect to handling of goods, and (c)
improving maintenance facilities (Chapter V).
Improvina Transport Planning, Programming md Budgeting
1.33     The transport investment planning, programming and budgeting
mechanism deserves considerable improvement, both at agency and ministerial
levels. The public investments have not been consistently based on
adequate feasibility studies, and there exists no mechanism for review,
monitoring and intermodal coordination. The Groupe o'Etude des Transports
(GET), a transport study group intended as planning arm of the Ministry of
Transports and Communications, has been used mainly as an advisory body to
the Minister on operational matters and has therefore not been able to
function as a long term planning and coordination instrument. A
reorganization study is now underway, and the Government has agreed to
review its recommendations and start implementing them by early 1989.
Consistency of Transport Policy with Country Development
1.34     Zaire's transport strategy has to be consistent with the
development goals of the country. While transport is a known bottleneck to
agricultural development in Zaire's hinterland, the current trade policy
does not encourage the consumption of local products but rather favors food
imports which have aggravated in recent years Zaire's dependency. Food
imports which represent 57% of total tonnage at Mata6i in 1987 compared to
29% in 1975 (Table 1.09) are apparently neither discouraged by the rate of
the zaire's devaluation nor by customs duties that are low on basic food
stuff (Table 1.10). The continued trade dependency on South Africa which
products represent 70X of Zaire's imports in the region is another factor



- 11 -
that perpetuates obstacles to the non-economic integration of the country
(Table 1.11). Efforts to improve the VN should stimulate local production
and help develop self-sufficiency.
Funding of OR
1.35     OR has suffered chronically from inadequate funding, a problem
Government resolved in 1985 by substartially increasing the amount of road
tax on fuel. The funding problem has becoms very acute again: from May
1987 (date of the last partial tax increase) until January 1989 the road
tax has not been readjusted despite a ministerial decree in May 1988 which
was not endorsed at higher level; other steps to ensure prompt payment of
the tax, and to peg it to inflation have in principle been agreed to, but
have yet to be implemented and OR's finances are still in a very critical
stage. The issue was raised under the SAC and assurances have yet to be
given to IDA that payment due to OR will be resumed. In the meantime,
disbursements on the Sixth Highways Project (Cr. 1608-ZR) have been
formally suspended on May 30, 1988. At the time of putting this report to
press, major adjustmeiics to fuel prices have been announced by decree of
January 31, 1989 to be effective February 1. The increase of fuel tax is
appropriate to fund OR and OR's resources would now be based on an ad
valorem tax.
The Launching of a Rural Road Improvement Program
1.36     About 110,000 km of the road network is entrusted to provincial
and local authorities, and receives only minor and sporadic upkeep
undertaken under local and/or external project financing and with OR
technical assistance. A program to establish an appropriate institutional
framework for rural road development has been initiated under the new Rural
Development Ministry and the Agricultural Road Service (SNRDA).   The
program is initially designed to maintain an additional 10,000 km of rural
roads per year with about 3000 km of rehabilitation to be subsequently
maintained. A full scale project is being prepared with UNDP financing and
with IDA as executing agency, for implementation From 1989.
Rehabilitation of RVA and the Improvement of Airport Infrastructure and
Equipment
1.37     The aviation subsector plays a crucial role in Zaire freight and
passenger transport but has been very neglected in the past. Due to lack
of funds, severe deterior&tion is occuring in airport infrastructure and
equipment, particularly at Kinshasa and Lubumbashi where the runways
require urgent repair. RVA also requires institutional strengthening.
F. IDA*s Role and Strategy in the Sector
1.38     IDA's role in the transport sector in Zaire has been important and
determinant. Since Zaire's independence in 1960, IDA has helped finance 13
transport projects for a total of $355.5 million. These comprised 6
highways projects , one river transport, two railway projects, two
rail/river projects, one port project and one river navigation project.
Three Projects Performance Audits have been carried out and two reports
have been issued, one for the First Highway Project (1978), and the second
jointly for the Second and Third Highway Projects and for the River
Transport and First Rail/River Projects (October 7, 1983). The main



- 12 -
conclusions, confirmed by experience from more recent projects such as the
Fourth Highway Project (for which a PCR was issued on January 31, 1985) are
that: (i) the projects were too much focused on physical investments and
not enough on managerial and institutional weaknesses. The difficult
environment ard deficiencies in implementation caused only part of the
projects' objectives to be met; a number of financial covenants were not
respected; (ii) some institutional developments were introduced such as the
launching of new agencies (OR, RVF and RVM). Large numbers of Zairean
employees were trained. But the audit indicates that serious problems
remained concerning the size, responsibilities and strategies of ONATRA and
OR; (iii) the importance of technical assistance is vital in the
management of ONATRA and OR. The replacement of expatriates by Zairean
staff must be pursued while ensuring the availability of adequate technical
resources for management; (iv) the institutions responsible for policy
making and control should be strengthened in parallel with the main
transport agencies; and (v) investment priorities should be analyzed within
the context of the entire transport system, rather than in relation to one
major transport mode.
1.39     The results on recent and ongoing IDA projects are generally
encouraging (ONATRA Cr. 1180-ZR and 1335-ZR) from the stand point of
project implementation, although in some cases major delays have occured
(SNCZ Cr. 902-ZR and 1475-ZR). However, recent managerial appointments in
both institutions have given positive results by speeding up physical
implementation, in particular of a large locomotives and wagons
rehabilitation program at SNCZ. Financial performance have been
disappointing both at ONATRA and SNCZ, mainly due to the impact of
inflation that has driven operating costs up much faster than revenues, but
also because costs saving measures have not been identified early enough.
Financial deficits are large and for ONATRA a capital restructuring measure
was just approved to restore the debt: equity ratio to manageable
proportion and enable resumption of debt service payments.
1.40     The central role of ONATRA and SNCZ in the transport sector and
the necessary intermodal complementarity do not allow a fragmentation of
these two parastatals as the private sector cannot present a viable
alternative at the moment. Given the willingness of Government to carry
out major reforms in the public enterprise sector, including selective
privatization of services, IDA has an important role to play in continuing
to support such reforms, and in helping to mobilize and coordinate
cofinanciers assistance.
1.41     IDA is presently involved in all transport subsectors and
parastatals except for aviation (RVA and Air Zaire), shipping (CMZ) and
private road transport. Dialogue with Government has been intensified over
the past few years and is being pursued through different channels: (i)
agency-specific and investment related issues are being discussed in the
context of seven individual ongoing projects; (ii) a Transport Sector
Memorandum has been discussed with Government in 1986; (iii) a cofinancing
meeting in Paris in March 1988 for the transport sector; and (iv) sector
issues and the transport investment program have been discussed in the
context of the SAC and the 1987 and 1988 PIP reviews. Bank assistance has
been effective in mobilizing the required external financing for the sector
and it has a major role to play in coordinating donors programs. The main
sectoral issues and development objectives indicated above have been agreed
with Government and will be followed up by IDA using the various lending or
dialogue instruments available.



- 13-
II. OFFICE NATIONAL DES T,ANSPORTS (ONATRA)
A. Organization and Management
2.01     ONATRA was cstablished in 1971 to su ceed OTRACO, created in 1935,
as the autonomous governmental agency which provides railways, port, and
river transport services mainly on the Voie Nationale (para. 1.1") and on
the Zaire River and its tributaries.
2.02     ONATRA is organized under:  (i) the 1978 legislation governing all
public entreprises; (ii) the 1978 Ordinance constituting its statues; and
(iii) a 1971 licensing by-law (Cahier des Charges), enumerating in detail
the services it provides, its obligations thereof and the rights and
obligations of the licensing authority, i.e., Government. These
instruments reflect the concept that ONATRA is to provide a transport
service in the public interest, as a regulated public service, operating in
line with commercial practices. This concept (i) governs the distribution
of tasks, responsibilities and financing between Government and ONATRA;
(ii) places ONATRA under supervision of the Commissaire d'Etat aux
Transports; and (iii) submits ONATRA to some of the statutes applicable to
Government Departments, mainly to Government contract law. The present
legislation, statues and by-laws do not hinder ONATRA's management, but
they will be appropriately updated when the on-going and proposed reforms
are implemented (Chapter III).
2.03     ONATRA's Board of Directors has nine members, including a
Chairman/President; all are appoint"d by the President of the Republic.
ONATRA's PDG has great authority. The present organization has three
operational (Ports, Railways, River Transport including shipyards) and
seven support departments iFinance, General Administration including
Personnel, Police, Procurement, Legal, Commercial, Building services or
Regie des Travaux) which report directly to the President; this
organization is thus highly centralized (Chart 3).
2.04     The deterioration of ONATRA's performance has accelerated in the
last two years and may be partially linked to management changes which
occurred in 1985 and partially to many other factors. Notable among them
are the lack of flexibility of management to adapt to changing conditions
in the transport sector and the poor service provided to its clients. It
faces increasing competition in competitive sectors (road between Matadi
and Kinshasa and river transpcort activities).
B. Port. Railway and River Transport Facilities
Matadi
2.05     Matadi, located 140 km from the ocean, is Zaire's main seaport,
accounting for more than 90% of total non petroleum maritime trade. The
only other maritime port of any significance is Boma, located about 30 km
downstream from Matadi. Petroleum products are handled at Banana, near
Zaire's refinery, and at another terminal near Matadi. Navigation to
Kinshasa, 350 km upstream of Matadi, is not possible because of river
rapids. At present, water depth is limited to about 7.2 m in the meandering
passes of the Zaire river, forcing either the use of small ships fully
laden (5,000 to 10,000 rWo') or larger ships partially laden. The ongoing
Navigation Improvement prnJect (Cr. 1761-ZR) aims to increase available



- 14-
depth to 8.6 m, which would allow most ships serving the area to transit
under full load (about 20,000 DWT).
2.06     Matadi has ten berths, three of which have recently been renovated
under the ongoing Ports Rehabilitation Project (Cr. 1335-ZR). Three of the
others are in relatively good and four are in very poor condition. The port
is equipped to handle containers (using ship's gear for ship-shore
transfer), bulk grain, refined metals, lumber and general cargo.
Operations are difficult because of the limited width available between the
waterfront and the steep hills on either side of the river valley (150 to
200 m) and the rail lines and marshalling yards in the port area.
2.07     Transit sheds on the downstream berths are in good condition;
those upstream are no longer used. Rolling surfaces are generally adequate
in about 60% of the port area and need paving and/or earthworks in the
rest. Vehicular access is difficult, and parking space and new access works
are needed. The existing container storage area would be adequate if the
containers remained only a few days in the port as intended. Major
improvements are needed to entrance barriers, security enclosures and
maintenance facilities.
2.08     Equipment poses a maintenance and productivity problem, which no
additions will solve if not accompanied by management and personnel
improvements. The productivity of the almost new Matadi container terminal,
which, if not good, was at least acceptable in 1986, became very low in
1987. New handling equipment, including tractors, tire mounted cranes and
forklifts saw drops in productivity levels one or two years after delivery,
afflicted by lack of spare parts, severe damage, and frequent breakdowns.
Kinshasa
2.09     The port, adjacent to downtown Kinshasa extends for about 1800
linear meters of waterfront. Though Kinshasa is a focal point of
practically all river traffic to and from the interior of Zaire, ONATRA's
port share of this traffic has decreased while that of other private river
ports (denominated "Beaches") has increased. Originally, a rail-water
transfer port, ONATRA's port was profoundly changed by the on-going Ports
Rehabilitation Project; it has now two long-reach-heavy lift container/log
handling cranes with relatively vast storage areas. In addition, there are
new river-craft mooring facilities and some of its piers have been
renovated.
Matadi-Kinshasa Railway
2.10     The Chemin de Fer Matadi-Kinshasa (CFMK), completed in 1898, spans
365 kms betveen the port and the capital, traversing rolling terrain with
numerous curves, gradients and bridges. Track renewal was begun under Cr.
571-ZR, and is continuing under the Onatra Modernization Project, Cr.
1180-ZR, but 180 kms remain to be renewed. The track is generally In
stable condition without unduly long or severe speed restrictions. The
incidence of rail breakages, rail table defects and rail wear, however,
calls for a phased continuation of the renewal work. ONATRA has signed a
contract under an Italian credit for 50kms of track renewal.
2.11     The system's all-diesel motive power consists of 25 main line
locomotives and 30 shunters. The mainline locomotives, all 1500 hp,
comprise 12 ALCOs (1952), 8 Krupp U-15Cs (1982), and 5 Krupp U-15Cs (1987).



- 15-
Availability has been poor, with figures of 43.1% for 1986 and 51.6% for
1987 which includes five months of service of 5 new locomotives. The 12
Alcos are due to be scrapped in the course of the next few years. A CCCE
credit in 1987 has just financed 7 new main line Alsthom-locos of 2400 hp,
and these are expected to be delivered during 198811989.
2.12     The 30 shunting locomotives comprise:  (i) 13 Baume and Marpent
(1952) of which 12 have been re- ngined with Deutz engines; (ii) 7 Toshiba
(1969), all re-engined; (iii) 6 d.U.F. (1958), re-engined with M.T.U
engines; and (iv) 4 GE-U-10-B (1977) (shunting/transfer services).
Availauility has been around 60%. A Belgian credit is due to finance 5
shunting locomotives and 4 shunting/transfer locos, and these are expected
to be delivered during 1988 and 1989. Locomotives maintenance facilities
at Mbanza-Ngungu need overhaul.
2.13     The wagon fleet on the system consists of 2,752 wagons for
commercial services, of which 2,312 are over 30 years old. 195 of the
latest wagons (containers and flats) are equipped with air-brakes. The
availability of wagons is 70%. Under Cr. 1180-ZR, 302 wagons have been
modified and fitted with air-brakes and 498 more kits received are to be
installed during 1988. The fleet of 150 coaches, of which 81 are 50 years
old has an availability of 59%. A CCCE credit in 1987 has provided funds
for the replecement of 36 bogies and the rehabilitation of 29 coaches to
operate one express and one regular passenger trains in each direction
between Kinshasa and lAatadi. Maintenance facilities located in Kinshasa
suburbs are sufficient.
2.14     The existing telecommunications system, based on a cable system
more than 30 years old, extends between Kinshasa and Lufu-Toto and another,
setup in 1976, between Lufu-Toto and Matadi. The former is unreliable, and
the latter does not meet required standards. The 35km long Kinshasa-
Sonabata section works on a centralized traffic control system over 30
years old; this is starting to become unreliable. The rest of the line
uses Webb-Thompson instruments, which are obsolescent.
River Transport
2.15     ONATRA owns and operates on the main navigation axis a fleet of
large river craft including about 35 pushers/tugs and 150 barges. Although
most of the equipment is old and in poor condition, 8 new 1350 HP pushers
and 35 new 600 ton barges, acquired under recent projects, should permit
ONATRA to scrap an important amount of obsolete equipment; which in turn
should improve operating efficiency and coats. The renovation program
should continue on a much more limited scale as a means to improve rotation
time. ONATRA also owns and operates a fleet of small pushers and barges
(150-600 HP, 80-150 tons) serving a complex netwo:k of tributaries. Most
of this equipment is also old and in poor condition, but it is not
considered justified at this time to renovate it.
2.16     The shipyardfi at Ndolo, Mbandaka, and Boma have recently been
incorporated into the River Operations Department, to render the department
responsible for the state of its fleet, and to stop the vandalism and theft
on the units about to be sent to the shipyards. Despite their relatively
good infrastructure and equipment, the shipyaras are very inefficient and
their productivity is very low; their divestiture and eventual
privatization is under consideration (para. 3.14).



- 16-

recent years as summarized below:

2.18     Although traffic haa been growing in Matadi port (except in 1987),
it is declining in other ONATRA departments. The causes of this trend are
the deterioration of the services and capacity of ONATRA's system has
discouraged many customers, who have developed their own transport or have
resorted to other private operators and the general drop in consumption by
urban dwellers that has affected the rural areas whose production in recent
years has decreased.
2.19     Matadi port presently handles about 1.5 million tons per year; its
capacity could increase to about 2.5 million tons if improvemesnts are made
in equipment, infrastructure, and in management and operating methods,
including simplification of customs procedures. If port traffic continues
to grow, it will then become necessary to undertake major works to increase
capacity or to transfer traffic to the nearby Boma port.
2.20     Kinshasa port traffic has averaged about 600,000 tons p.a. for
several years while its new design capacity about to be completed is about
1.5 million tons. The major issue facing the port is how to effectively
utilize the new infrastructure investments since neither container nor log
traffic has developed as expected. A profitable use of these investments
could be to: (i) use the container terminal as an inland container
terminal, and not just as a rail-water transfer point; and (ii) attract
private traffic currently using private "beaches', to ONATRA's port.
ONATRA has decided to open the port to the private sector and during
negotiations assurances were obtained that this will be implemented in
accordance with an agreed schedule as part of the reorganization (paras.
3.05 and 8.02).
2.21     CFMK railway traffic has fallen from 2.72 million tons in 1957 to
1 million in 1987. Some sections of track will need replacing in the long
term, but the track is adequate for present forecast traffic. The main
factors limiting CFMK's performance are poor availability of locomotives
and wagons, low rotation of rolling stock, and poor quality of service.
These will be adressed by improving maintenance, operational and commercial
efficiency under the proposed project (para. 2.23).



- 17-
2.22     ONATRA's river traffir has also fallen, from over 1 million tons
in 1976 to about 670,000 tons .. 1'987. Its share of traffic has fallen
from 80% in 1980 to 50% today. however, there is potential for ONATRA to
develop its services and regain some traffic. ONATRA is amply equipped to
handle extra traffic, but there is a need to replace some barges to be
scrapped, by standard 600 tons units to complete the convoys for copper and
by specialized barges for traders-passenger transport.
D. 2perations and Efficiency
2.23     ONAThA's operating efficiency improved slightly between 1984 and
1987, except foc: (i) wagon turn-around time and availability in CFMK
railway; (ii) container handling at Matadi; and (iii) rotation of available
river convoys. Further, a large number of recent investmevts are far from
yielding their design productivity. The proposed project aims at reducing
the total transit time for mineral exports from Shaba to Matadi, which is a
good overall indicator of the efficiency of the Voie Nationale. The main
efforts to improve transport time will concentrate on the connections
(transhipments at Ilebo and Kinshasa; waiting time in CFMK yards, etc.).
An operational target of 20 to 25 days (from the current 43 days at the end
of 1987) for the Shaba-Matadi journey seems achievable with proper
management of operations, assuming that the equipment is in reasonably good
condition, and an efficient training program is carried out. The break-
down of current ar.d proposed transit time between the various sections of
the Voie Nationale are shown in Table 2.01. The proposed project will
address above deficiencies through a number of measures defined in an
Operational Action Plan (Annex 1).  This Plan, ela,orated jointly by ONATRA
and SNCZ, recognizes areas for raising performance and achieving agreed
targets. Measures will be implemented under an agreed timetable. During
negotiations, assurances were obtained that the Action Plan will be
finalized and sent to the Association under a letter of policy jointly by
the PDGs of ONATRA and SNCZ, committing the two agencies to implement it
during the project life (para. 4.18). This document was received early in
October 1988, and this met one of the conditions of Board Presentation
(para. 8.01). The implementation of this Action Plan in accordance with an
agreed timetable is a condition of the Credit and Project Agreements (para.
8.02).
2.24     Matadi port handles about 500 ships per year, the majority being
conventional general cargo or polyvalent type. Full container ships,
however, are calling regularly. In 1981, the port handled on average 505
tons/ship/day which compared favorably with most Sub-Saharan ports. In
1986, this number exceeded 800; thus (except for container handling) Matadi
continues to rank relatively well in Africa.
2.25     There are three shifts per day with optional work on Sundays and
holidays. Because there is no night navigation (limited night navigation is
an objective of the Navigation Improvement Project) ships that cannot sail
by 13:30 hours must wait until 05:30 hours the next morning. This
restriction, in turn, induces use of night shifts to reduce time at port.
Overall berth occupancy was 61% in 1981 and in 1987 in spite of traffic
increases, due to gains in productivity of general cargo handling and to
containerization.
2.26     Container handling efficiency s.t Matadi declined severely from 213
TEU/shiplday in 1986 to 122 in 1987 (Table 2.2 (T)). Though ONATRA lacked
spare parts for tractors, this is due to poor management of container



- 18-
operations, given the improvement in equipment and infrastructure procured
under Cr. 1180-ZR. The departure of expatriate technical assistance
affected negatively the operations of the Matadi Container Terminal. The
proposed project will provide technical assistance to improve container
handling.

2.27     Except for pilotage and dredging which are carried out by RVM,
port functions are carried out by ONATRA. Nonetheless, a "de-facto"
privatization of stevedoring by owners has occasionally occurred in the
handling of some foodstuffs in order to reduce ship time at port. The port
employs about 2,800 people, and although the Zairean port manager is
competent, there is a severe shortage of qualified high level personnel.
2.28     Kinshasa port handles about 1500 barges a year; its productivity
is about 100 tons/barge/day, lower than the ongoing project targets (Cr.
1335-ZR), but this is partly due to the interference produced by
construction work (Table 2.3 (T)). Maintenance and cargo handling
operations are worse than at Matadi, and the senior management of the port
needs to be upgraded. Security is a serious problem exacerbated by
passenger traffic (ferries to Brazzaville and convoys to the interior).
The opening of the port to private operators and the establishment of a
Capitainerie de Port to handle river craft movement are included in the
proposed project.

bilateral assistance for maintenance management should improve traction
capacity. The support for operations management proposed in the project
will address the need to improve asset utilization.

Z.  Human Roures and Training
2.33     ONATRA had some 18,300 staff in 1987, a reduction of 72 from 1982,
and from some 26,000 in the late 60s. There are about 80 expatriates, down
from 300 in the 1970's. However, large staff increases took place in
administrative departments, while staff in prodACtive departments has
declined (Table 2.02). Some units are now depleted of qualified staff
because retirees have not been replaced. Also, managerial staff is very
low in technical and high in administrative departments. The
foremen/workers ratio is very high at 412, reflecting promotions on a
seniority basis and overgrading. Only 302 of managers have higher or
university education; engineers are only 52 of managerial staff. The
professional level of foremen and workers is critically low; a systematic
evaluation of 3,000 staff found only 602 of them with qualifications
consistent with their positions.
2 34     ONATRA'n management encourages retirement at age 55.  A recent
decision has been accepted by Unions and Government to retire automatically
staff aged 55 years with 30 years of service, at generous conditions. This
will permit the replacement of aged employees by better educated young
staff. The risk of losing valuable, well trained staff should be avoided
through careful monitoring. Under the project ONATRA intends to accelerate
the retirement of staff. During negotiations, agreement was reached for
establishing a retirement fund to which IDA will contribute in support of a
reduction of some 2000 employees during 1989 (para. 3.06, 5.04 and 8.02).
2.35     Despite management efforts, ONATRA has remained weak in job
analysis, recruitment procedures, promotion systems, staffing control,
monitoring of personnal movements and manpower planning in general. The
proposed project includes the necessary studies and technical assistance to
reinforce the personnel function in the agency (par&. 3.07).
2.36     ONATRA maintains six specialized training centers (Shipyard,
Navigation, Railways, Matadi Port, Administration and Police), all under
the Directorate of Human Resources at Headquarters. Following an IDA-
financed study by consultants and ONATRA's own initiatives, staff months of
training nearly trebled between 1984 and 1986. There is still, however, a
major need to train managers. A 1988-1992 training plan has been oet up,
and support in its execution will be part of the proposed project. During
negotiations, the Government, ONATRA and the Association have agreed on
ONATRA's training plan and on its progrem of execution, to be carried out
ir.  :sultation with IDA (par& 8.02).



- 21-
F. Tariffs and Costs
2.37     ONATRA's current tariff system results from historic constraints
(ad valorem tariffs), exceptions, negotiations, etc. Adjustments are
necessary to bring them closer to the real costs, to allow for fair
competition, optimal allocation of traffic and overall efficient economy,-
Table 2.03 compares freight costs and tariffs for CFMK, showing that ONA,i'R
subsidizes the transport of logs and agricultural products, although it: I's
overpaid for containers. Tariff adjustments, for which past variatiolu6
were quite erratic in constant terms (Table 2.04), are gradually made ai:
ONATRA. In 1986, ONATRA and SNCZ obtained the indexation of Gecamines
mineral traffic and are seeking the indexation of other general freight
Also, ONATRA has already increased some symbolic tariffs, such as those of
Deluxe and First Class in river services. Moreover, ONATRA is completinr, a
study on tariff structure based partly on the cost accounting system anw to
be implemented in 1989. During negotiations, ONATRA agreed that its net;
tariff structure will be implemented by June 1989 (para 8.02). Also,
Government agreed with the Association that the Contrat-Prograrmme being
prepared will aim at cost based tariffs and will vest the tariff setting
responsibility in ONATRA (para. 3.09, 5.04 and 8.04).
2.38     While ONATRA's overall unit operating costs, in constant zair>s,
have increased by more than 30Z between 1980 and 1986, CFMK railway
operating costs jumped by 70Z and those of Kinshasa port by 210%. The
Matadi port and river transport operating costs increased more reasonably
(respectively 23% and 18Z) (Table 1.04). Many external factors help to
explain these changes: drop in traffic, increase in inputs costs,
inaccuracy in accounting data. But internal reasons linked to
mismanagement may also have played a role.   For instance, fuel consumption
in physical terms (cubic meters per traffic unit) increased by 26% from
1980 to 1986 in river transport (Table 2.05). Overall administrative costs
have also penalized ONATRA's departments, as illustrated by general
administration staff increase. Controls on consumables are to be anfozced
as part of ONATRA's financial recovery and reorganization plans (paras 2.46
and 7.03).
G. Financial Management
Orxanization
2.39     ONATRA's financial management suffers from the same organiza-i-onal
problems as general management; it is over-centralized and cumbersome;
staff are underpaid and lacking in motivation and experience (Chart 3).
The lack of delegation of responsibility to middle levels of management and
the poor salary levels have severely constrained ONATRA's ability to
recruit or retain staff of the right calibre.
2.40     However, expatriate staff at key levels (including that of
financial controller) have, through on-the-job training and the transfeL- o.
know-how, helped to improve the competence of Zaireans and to some extcnt.
compensated for these shortcomings: financial information produced for
management is generally comprehensive, reliable and up-to-date.
2.41     To function efficiently, a centralized organizational structu,.e
requires complex and reliable information transmission and internal control
systems which are lacking. In addition, motivated and capable management
is needed to operate them.   The present lack of responsible middle-level



- 22-
management in ONATRA and the difficult operating environment in Zaire have
led to serious weaknesses in the transmission of information and in
internal controls being reported by the external auditors. The correction
of such weaknesses and the improved efficiency of ONATRA's financial
management is one of the major objectives of the reorganization/
decentralization of ONATRA under the proposed project (para. 3.12).
Systems
2.42     ONATRA's financial accounting systems are based on the Zairean
national accounting plan (Plan Comptable Zairois-PCZ) for commercial
enterprises, which respects basic accounting concepts and allows compliance
with international accounting standards. Annual accounts are produced for
each calendar year and audited in accordance with international auditing
guidelines by independent auditors acceptable to the IDA.  Summarized
audited accounts for the years 1983 to 1986 are given in Table 7.01.
2.43     Management accounting in ONATRA is also comprehensive.  Detailed
monthly management accounts are produced within acceptable delays for each
operating department. Annual budgets are prepared in advance of the
previous year end and revised half-yearly; the monthly management accounts
compare actual performance with budgeted figures. Five-year forecasts are
also available. However, the reasonably efficient functioning of the
management accounting system is largely dependent on services controlled or
provided by expatriate staff, namely cost accounting and budget, computer
services and the management accounting department itself.
Accounts and Audit
2.44     The reliability of ONATRA's accounting records is shown by the
fact that for several years the auditors have not, with certain exceptions
for 1985 and 1986,1 qualified their opinion of the view presented by the
annual accounts. However, interim audit reports have regularly pointed to
serious weaknesses in internal controls and information transmission
systems; remarks that have, according to the auditors, largely been
unheeded by ONATRA. The likely implications of these weaknesses are (i)
the loss of revenue from unrecorded traffic and (ii) excessive expenditures
from wasteful and/or fraudulent consumption of supplies and services.
Cost and Management Accounting
2.45     The over-riding weakness of the management accounting system is
the absence of a reliable cost-accounting system on which to base it;
although a detailed cost accounting system exists, costs are analyzed by
cost center rather than by units of production and are not established
directly from the accounting records. Consultants, in addition to the
reorganization study, are carrying out a study of ONATRA's tariffs; this
study has (partly through necessity) been developed to include the setting
1/ Qualifications in 1985 and 1986 were principally linked to the changes
in values resulting from evaluation of (i) fixed assets and the charge for
depreciation resulting therefrom and (ii) long-term debt and the provision
for exchange losses resulting therefrom, and the accounting treatment of

- 23-
up of a cost-accounting system as an extension of the general accounting
system (to ensure continuity and reliability) and based on units rather
than centers of production.
Proposed Improvements
2.46     The establishment under the proposed project of free-standing
accounting systems for each operating department of ONATRA, as part of the
decentralization of ONATRA's management, should greatly simplify internal
control requirements, improve the exhaustiveness, timeliness and
reliability of the accounting records and also facilitate the establkshment
of adequate cost and management-accounting information. This will provide
management with the means necessary for efficient monitoring and control of
operations and safeguarding of assets.  At the same time the greater
responsibility enjoyed by departmental management should provide some of
the additional motivation necessary to ensure that the new systems are
adhered to.
B.  Planning and Investment
2.47     ONATRA's development strategy is supported by a program of limited
investments which has two main objectives: (i) improving operating
efficiency, especially in those activities that should generate cash and
(ii) meeting the financial constraints which limit its investment capacity.
ONATRA's proposed investment plan (1988-92) is given in Table 2.06.
2.48     This plan, totalling US$ 161 million for the 1988-91 period is
not consistent, however, with the March 1988 PIP review that has
limited ONATRA's investments to Z13,544 (or $121 million) for
1988-1991.  The discrepancy is largely due to earlier credit facilities
which are only to be utilized in part.  Also, some components of the
plan will be delayed, as has been observed in previous programs. The
total investment plan (1988-92) is about US$100 million more than the
proposed project, which accounts for US$78 million.  A disbursement
rate of 70% of this plan, which is consistent with ONATRA's previous
investment performance, would imply investments of $140 million between
1988 and 1992 which is more in line with the PIP and thus is
acceptable.  Investments have been included in the financial forecasts
on this basis (Tables 7.01 to 7.04). During negotiations, it was
agreed that the Investment Plan of ONATRA will be reviewed annually in
the context of the overall Public Expenditures Program (PEP) review of
the Government, with the objective of limiting investment commitments
within the PEP ceiling (para. 8.02).
