III. ONATRA'S INSTITUTIONAL REFORM
A. Perforrmnce and the Need for Institutional Reform
3.01     ONATRA's internal performance has been poor during recent years.
Paras 2.23 to 2.32 give details on poor operational performance and its
causes. Paras 2.33 to 2.36 analyze the staffing problems and Paras 2.37 to
2.46 deal with the inadequacy of tariffs and of financial management.
3.02     ONATRA's performance has also been affected by exogenous causes:
(i) it is part of a public enterprise and public management system where
performance is uneven, motivation is poor, and which does not at present
enjoy the reputation of strong ethics and good service to users; (ii)
tariffs are set by Government and increases granted in recent years have
not offset inflation; and (iii) the performance of other government
agencies in the transport sector, such as Customs, directly affects
ONATRA's own performance or its interests.
3.03     Changes, however, have started on Government's and ONATRA's
initiative. A new general manager was appointed in November 1987, with
considerable management experience of large organizations. His efforts
have started to immediately improve the enterprise's performance meeting
operational objectives. Assisted by consultants, he is also continuing
preparation of ONATRA's reorganization and management reforms on the scheme
outlined by his predecessor, under the name of Projet d'Entreprise. IDA's
preparation of the proposed project was conducted in parallel with ONATRA
and consultants' work. Under bilateral financing, ONATRA intends to secure
the services of consultants for helping to implement the reforms. Their
terms of reference are acceptable to IDA.  During negotiations, ONATRA and
the Government agreed that ONATRA will continue to implement their Proiet
d'Entreprise: 1988-1992 Actualisation-Programmation, issued in December
1987, modified from time to time as needed and in consultation with the
Association (para. 8.02).
B. Reform Obiectives
3.04     The basic objective of reforms is to improve ONATRA's performance
(i) to provide the Zairean economy and users with the most cost-effective
transport services and (ii) to stop the deterioration of ONATRA's
competitive position on the market segment on which it intends to remain.
There needs to be (i) improvement in staffing and personnel management;
(ii) changes in ONATRA's model of relations with Government, to clarify
their respective roles and responsibilities; (iii) changes in internal
organization, basically to decentralize all ONATRA's departments; and (iv)
changes in modes of operations of each of ONATRA's departments, such as the
divestiture of ONATRA's selected activities as an own account operatnr
where its direct intervention is not the best modus operandi.
3.05     All four institutional/organizational changes are part of ONATRA's
institutional reform as proposed in ONATRA's Pro1et d'Entreprise. In its
Implementation Action Plan issued in February 1988, which is acceptable to
IDA, the Prolet d'Entreprise includes six programs and related objectives.
These ares (1) decentralization of management; (2) development of
information systems and of their management; (3) study and reform of
auxiliary services; (4) study and reform of non-commercial services; (5)



- 25 -
preparation of a Contrat-Programme; and (6) the opening of ONATRA's
Kinshasa river port as a ?ublic port. Changes are outlined below, and an
Institutional Reform Action Plan with monitorable targets has been set up
(Annex 2).
C.  Improvement in StaffinR
3.06     Staff attrition (para 2.33), still in progress, will not solve all
ONATRA's staffing problems. The large number of under-qualified and aged
staff makes their retraining unproductive. The on-going retirement program
will be accelerated, and ONATRA agreed at negotiations to the retirement of
some 2,000 workers in 1989 with IDA providing financial support for a
retirement fund to cover expenditures (paras. 2.34, 5.04, 5.15 and 8.02).
The Human Resources Department will be restructured and its terms of
reference redefined; this is part of the overall reorganization which will
decentralize personnel management to line departments, the central office
having mainly a staff role (para 3.11). The definition of recruitment,
personnel management and promotion procedures will be conducted after basic
reorganization end redistribution of departments has taken place (Annex 3).
3.07     As regards technical assistance for which the Bank carried out a
special survey in October 1987, ONATRA needs (i) to develop its capability
to manage and coordinate its programs and (ii) to strengthen its personnel
mAnagement and training systems to create the conditions for a phased
replacement of expatriates. Needs for TA are under review; some new
positions are required while internal movements are necessary. A special
accent needs to be placed on the training role o. technical assistants.
The proposed project includes technical assistance for (i) implementing the
reorganization reforms; (ii) improving operations; (Ii) supervision of
rehabilitation, construction, maintenance and works reinforcing the
personnel function; and (v) implementing the training program (para 5.04
and Annex 4). During negotiations, the Government, ONATRA and the
Association agreed on present technical assistance needs, recruiting and
sources of financing (para 8.02). In addition, it was agreed that any
amount, presently allocated to technical assistance, if not needed for
financing such technical assistance, would be allocated to training.
D. Chanaes in ONATRA's Relations with Government: Contrat-Proaramme
3.08     The present 1971 Licensing By-laws (para 2.02), issued before the
1978 Ordinance on Public Entreprises and, as such, already somewhat
outdated in relation to basic legislation, do not reflect adequately (i)
the dual role of the agency as a provider of public services and as a
commercial entity nor (ii) that relations between Government and ONATRA
should be based on mutual agreement as well as on statutes. The existing
statutes ought to be complemented by a Contrat-Programme. Under such a
contract: (i) Government would assign objectives to the agency for a
preliminary period of three years and (ii) Government and ONATRA would
agree on Government policies regarding ONATRA. Issues to be identified in
the Contrat-Programme will be (i) on ONATRA's side, those of financial
performance, staffing policies, marketing, operational performance, and
investments; (ii) on Government's side, balance sheet and debt
restructuring, compensation for unprofitable services, tariffs, policies,
bad debts of Government agencies to ONATRA; (iii) proc4dures for monitoring
performance under the contract.



- 26 -
3.09     It is important that the Contrat-Programme be not only a business-
oriented management document bit also reflect a new style of relations,
based on mutual agreement rather than on statutory proviaions, between
Government and its agency, and on the attainment of mutually-agreed
specific objectives. After the Contrat-Programme has been tested, the
licensing, if still considered necessary, ought to be updated. The outline
of the contract was reviewed with ONATRA. At negotiations, Government
agreed on entering into such a contract, which should be acceptable to IDA
(para. 5.04). The communication of this contract to the Association will
be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed credit (para 8.04). It was
also agreed that the contract will not be modified without consultations
with IDA and that IDA and ONATRA will consult one another annually on
contract execution (para. 8.02).
E.  Chantes in Internal Organization:  Decentralization
3.10     The present comb-like structure (Chart 3 of ONATRA) is no longer
adapted to the agency's environment. Decentralization should be thorough,
well accepted, and effective. The present Head Office has 4,000 staff,
that is 22% of total staff of 18,300. In broad terms, the financial
surplus produced by operating divisions is absorbed by Head Office
activities.
3.11     ONATRA will be decentralized.  The Departments (Railways, Matadi
Port, Kinshasa Port, River Transport/Secondary ports) will become quasi-
subsidiaries with a high degree of autonomy, each responsible for the
provision of a specific transport mode or type of activity. The Head
Office will (i) handle relations with Government and with Funding Agencies;
(ii) deal with broad policy issues for the ONATRA group; (iii) appoint
senior managers; (iv) control major investments; (v) provide investment and
other funds as required and monitor and control interdepartmental funding;
(vi) monitor attainment of objectives; and (vii) conduct research, planning
and studies, audit, management control and evaluation (Chart 4).
3.12     The Departments will be responsible for their operations,
financial management, marketing, personnel, procurement and the elaboration
of their development plans which will need to be approved by the Head
Office. They will maintain independent financial management systems
producing annual fina.ncial statements for consolidation in ONATRA. This
will necessitate the demarcation of OTATRA's activities and assets between
the different Departments, with the objective of future inter-departmental
billing for services rendered, and a major redeployment of staff and
positions. Chart 5 gives an outline of the new structure, which is
expected to be finalized by end-1988 and ready to be implemented in phases,
starting with experimentation of decentralization in the CFMK railway
during 1989.
F.  Chantes in Modes of Operations:  Devolution
3.13     ONATRA presently operates all its own services, that is (i)
transport services, such as river, rail, cargo-handling, (ii) support
services for its primarily revenue-earning activities, e.g. shipyards and
building maintenance; (iii) auxiliary services, such as security services,
i.e., cargo watch and iv) social services, such as discount food stores,
health and urban tr nsport for ONATRA's staff. To reach the above-defined
objectives of the project, it is advisable for ONATRA to concentrate on
those activities that it is better equippped to perform, leaving other



- 27 -
activities to other operators to perform, under license, lease, management
contract, privatization, any other form of divest'ture, in association with
ONATRA in mixed companies or independently. Since ONATRA's most urgent
problem is financial, divestiture should concentrate first (i) on loss-
making activities which another operator could clearly make profitable and
(ii) on support and other non-transport activities which are a drain on
ONATRA's resources and leave room for abuse. Social services demand
special treatment because of (i) existing legislation on the matter; (ii)
the need to consult with labor unions to avoid disturbances which may ruin
the reform; and (iii) of ONATRA's obligation to provide social services,
such as schools, in isolated locations.
3.14     ONATRA's Shipyard, the productivity of whtich is not commensurate
with its equipment and investments, is intended to be, for the time being,
a 100% subsidiary of ONATRA, thus isolating its management from the
agency's, with an interim plan (i) to limit investments to the minimum
necessary to conduct on-going operations and maintain existing capacity;
(ii) to establish cost-accounting and payment of services rendered to
ONATRA's other departments; and (iii) to program operations. ONATRA is
exploring the prospect of a joint-venture with an experienced international
shipyard operator. Consultants are about to be appointed for that purpose.
Merger with a local shipyard, a semi-private company in which ONATRA itself
has an interest, could be an alternative to present management. There are
fears, however, of a monopoly which would drive prices up. The scheme for
reform of the shipyards is expected to be operational in late 1989.
3.15     River Transport is at present unprofitab.e.  Its inadequate
services have led to a decline in its share of traffic as former customers
establish their own transport facilities. In the future, a clear
distinction is expected to be made between its three types of traffic.
First, the main copper-carrying/passenger transport line, between Kinshasa
and Ilebo, on the Zaire and Kasai will remain under ONATRA direct control,
as a vital system with high economic and pc'itical significance, on wlich
the agency will concentrate its best equipment and staff. An operational
plan, on which ONATRA ha'.s committed itself (para 2.23) aims at speeling up
turn-around of boats and cargo. Second, general cargo, agricultural
products, container and passenger services on the Zaire river between
Kinshasa and Kisangani which are exposed to strong competition will be
partly privatized by chartering ONATRA's equipment to private operators.
ONATRA has started to cut non-profitable services such as passengers to
direct demand towards the better equipped private sector. Third, local
public-service type, non-profitable services on the tributaries, after
their costs have been measured, will be proposed for compensation cr
suspension in the Contrat-Programme to be agreed with Government. However,
the main public service of ONATRA is its obligation to charge low tariffs
substantially below cost recovery level, for agricultural products
traasported by small traders to local retail markets (generally Kinshasa),
a social service to which public opinion and Government are sensitive.
3.16     Non-Transport Activities are distributed between (i) auxiliary
activities (Security, Police, Civil Works, Printshop, Plantations) whose
reorganization is entirely in the hands of management, (ii) social, staff-
benefit type activities which need to be reorganized in accordance with
labor legislation and in cooperation with Unions (Staff Transport, Housing,
Schools, Health, Discount Stores), and (iii) services to third-parties,



- 28 -
such as health services to isolated populations where ONATRA hac a
hospital. Reform of these activities will be conducted in two stagest (i)
review by consultants, in progress since March 1988 and (ii) implementation
of consultants' recommendations and ONATPA's decision in corsultation with
IDA. Cost/benefit analysis is a ma'Jr element of the revie. * Cost savings
is ,.he main objectivie. Solutions sought are decentralization to
operational units rather than present concentratinn at Headquarters, andlor
devolutioni to outside operators, whenever possible. During negotiations
agreement was obtained on the implementation timetable indicated in the
Action Plan shown in Annex 2 for ONATRA's Reorganization (para 8.02).
3.17     Security has too many staff, not enough equipment and irregular
ethics. It is to be decentralizd to each of the operational departments,
and the reduction in staffing, already in progress, will continue.
3.18     Civil Works are carried out by Regie des Travaux (RT).  RT will
cease any own account work except that part of daily maintenance which
cannot conveniently be contracted out. Other maintenance, large repairs
and new building work will be contracted out. The Ragie will be broken
into small units decentralized to each operational deprtment, as their
building maintenance 'snits. What will be left in Headquarters will be
organized as a self-accounting unit. Decentralizatization started in 1988.
Reorganization at Headquarters will be reviewed in 1988 for action in 1989.
3.19     Staff Transport has been discontinued except for staff on night
shifts. In accordance with Labor Law, staff are paid a transport
allowance. In addition to financial savings, this should result in
decreased absenteeism, so far allegedly caused by shortage of buses on
their assigned routes.
3.20     Housing is a major issue.  ONATRA has some 6,050 dwellings, of
which 680 are leased to other Government agencies, which owe ONATRA huge
amounts in rent arrears. An inventory is necessary, as well as a
revaluation of these major assets. A solution is devolution to a housing
company to be established as a joint venture. The matter is being reviewed
by consultants until January 1989.
3.21     The evaluation of the plantations will be conducted in 1988 with a
view to disposing of them. The devolution of the Discount Food Stores to
che Labor Unions is in progress. ONATRA is providing a cash advance to be
paid back monthly.
3.22     The provision of Health Services, a legal obligation to ONATRA as
regard to its staff, is a complex and sensitive issue for ONATRA's staff
and for those populations which benefit from those services in isolated
locations where they are not provided by Government. The matter is being
reviewed by consultants with a view (i) to obtain an inventory of what is
needed and of what exists; (ii) to identify what should be compensated for
in the contract with Government; and (iii) to reduce costs, and abuse of,
remaining services. Early results of the review show that management has
been especially poor and abuses numerous.



- 29 -
IV. SOCIETE RATIONALE DES CHEMINS DE FER ZAIROIS (SNCZ)
A. Oraanizetion and Management
4.01     SNCZ, a state-owned company, was formed in 1974 by merging five
existing rnilway companies operating two major networks and two river and
lake ports. The company is organized in line with (i) the 1978
legislations governing all public enterprises and (ii) the 1978 ordinance
constituting its statutes. Both documents are acceptable to IDA, but are
likely to be modified and improved when a new legislation on public
enterprises, following a UNDP-financed study and review by Government, will
be issued, probably in 1989. The present legislation is rather general and
was expected originally to be explicited in a licensing by-law (Cahiers des
Charges) which was never issued.
4.02     SNCZ's Chief Executive Officer is a President-Delegue General
appointed by the President of the Republic. The PDG serves also as
chairman of the Board of Directors. SNCZ's management reports to the MTC
on operational matters and to the MPF on its financial affairs.  Management
enjoys autonomy over its operations and finance. Following serious
problems in the early and mid-eighties of inadequate organization, low
staff morale, and controls and operational inefficiency, management has
been tightened, a new PDG appointed and technical assistance reinforced.
This was, with associated studies, one of the main items of the IDA
financed Zaire Second SNCZ Railway Project (Cr. 1475-ZR) at present under
execution. Details are given in Report 4779-ZR and subsequent supervision
reports on project.
4.03     The lack of licensing by-laws and the blurred distinction between
SNCZ's commercial transport activities and transport activities performed
in the public interest for social or macro-economic purposes, such as the
isolated, narrow-gauge, money losing Bumba-Mungbere network in the Haut
Zaire Region, make it necessary that relations between SNCZ and its only
shareholder, the Government of Zaire, be clarified. A Contrat-Programme
has been prepared to cover the 1989-1991 period. Its contents will be
generally similar to that of ONATRA'a contract (para. 3.09). The present
draft (July 1988) is satisfactory to the Association. During negotiations,
it was agreed that a Contrat, satisfactory to the Association, will be
entered into between Government and SNCZ. Communication of this contract
to the Association will be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed
Credit (para. 8.04). It was also agreed that the contract will not be
modified without consultations with IDA and that SNCZ and IDA will consult
one another annually on the contract's execution (para. 8.03).
B. Network and Transport Facilities
4.04     The SNCZ system has a total of 4742 km of single track, and
consists principally of 3,600 km of 1067mm gaugeline in the southern and
eastern areas of Zaire, servicing the mining complex, and linking: (a)
The Kasai/Zaire river navigation system at Ilebo at the northern end; (b)
Zambia and Angola at Sakania and Dilolo respectively in the south; and (c)
Tanzania via lake transport at the Port of Kalemie in the east (see Map).



- 30 -
4.05     A separate line of 600 mm gauge, 1021 km long, serves the
agricultural region in northeast Zaire. Another line of 1,000 mm gauge and
121 km long from Ubundu to Kisangani bypasses a non-navigable section of
the Zaire river. SNCZ also operates the transhipment ports of Ilabo and
Kalemie and some other minor ports, water transport services on the
Tanganyika and Kivu lakes, and road transport in eastern provinces. An
ongoing study is reviewing the low density lines to stem the large
financial losses such services generate by reducing them or by turning them
to the private sector. SNCZ has its headquarters in Lubumbashi, and
liaison offices in Kinshasa and Brussels (Belgium).
Track
4.06     Rails on the major portion of the VN are badly worn.  70 kms were
renewed in 1983 under the First SNCZ Railway Project (Cr. 902-ZR) and 128
kms of renewals were included in the Second SNCZ Project, of which 93 kms
have been completed. Out of the total length of 1,684 kms between
Kolwezi/Lubumbashi and Ilebo, 615 kms or 37% of the distance is under speed
restrictions of between 35 to 10 kpL. Funding for renewal of another 160
km was made available to SNCZ under the Second Project by the EEC, the AfDB
and by IDA; work is to be carried out during 1988-89. Over 750 kms of the
VN requires renewal during this decade, 303 kms of which have been
identified as priority renewal under the proposed project.
Signalling and Telecommunications
4.07     The signalling system over most of the VN is not reliable due to
its old age and it will need phased replacement. The telecommunications
system in the Central Region is being rehabilitated under the Second
Project. A technical study has been made for establishing the optimum
program for telecommunications works needed on the system, to be carried
out under this project.
Motive Power
4.08     Excluding the northern region, the locomotive fleet consists of 51
electric and 92 diesel main line and 59 diesel shunting locomotives. The
current availability is around 72% for electric, 48% for diesel main line
and 42% for shunting. Ten electric locomotives are of 2400 kw (Hitachi),
and the rest are of the 1500-1600 kw range (ACEC and Hitachi). Thirty
diesel locos are of 1800 HP (GE) purchased second hand in 1987 from the
South African Railways, 44 of 1650 HP (GE), 10 of 1550 HP (Hitachi) and 8
are of 1310 HP (Krupp).
4.09     Sixteen electric locomotives are 30 or more years old and are
currently used for shunting and departmental services, and will be
scrapped in the next three years. Thirty main line diesel locos are over
28 years old. Based on age-cum-condition, 22 locomotives are to be
scrapped in the next three years. Thirteen shunting locomotives, with only
280 hp and an availability of 29%, are used on the Eastern Region. Six out
of the remaining 46 locomotives are to be scrapped.
4.10     Motive power has been a limiting factor in the efficient operation
of the system. The spares import under the Second Project, coupled with
Technical Assistance and Management Support are being used for a large



- 31 -
repair and maintenance program, which is expected when completed late 1989
to improve the situation, but further imports of locomotives, spares, and
managemenit support are needed and included under the proposed project.
Rolling Stock
4.11      SNCZ has a fleet of 345 passenger coaches, with an availability of
542 and 5,108 commercial wagons including 728 belonging to other
orgAnizations, mainly Gecamines and the PETRO-ZAIRE, with an availability
of 71%.  Repair and rehabilitation of 850 wagons is betng carried out under
the Second Project. 264 low-sided container wagons are also being procured
and added to the fleet. More wagons need to be rehabilitated.
Workshops
4.12      The Central Workshop at Lubumbashi for diesel locomotives and the
workshop at Likasi for electric locomotives are in fair condition and
reasonably well-equipped. The Lubumbashi workshop also attends to wagons.
Port Facilities
4.13      The major port at Ilebo on the Kasai provides adequate
transhipment facilities for the traffic anticipated on the VN. The civil
works need major attention and SNCZ has included this work in their
investment program under bilateral aid. SNCZ has also an important port at
Kalemie on Lake Tanganyika.
C. Traffic and Capacities
4.14    Over the past ten years, passenger traffic has decreased more than
freight traffic, thanks to stable mineral traffic.  The 1987 decrease is
due to the lack of both traction and fuel.

4.15     The imbalance between imports and exports on the Ilebo-Shaba line
has grown out of the Government's insistence on the use of the line for
imineral exports, and the increased use of alternative routes for imports
owing to the line's poor reliability. The track renewal program proposed
under the project combined with more effective commercial policy will help
to return to a better load factor upstream. At negotiations, SNCZ agreed
to prepare a commercial action plan before January 1, 1989 (para. 8.03).
4.16     SNCZ's major physical constraints to be addressed in the proposed
project ares
(i)   the deteriorated condition of track caused by worn rails,
corroded sleepers, clogged ballast due to leakage of copper
concentrate from wagons, and by lack of systematic
maintenance. Apart from numerous speed restrictions and line
blockages resulting from the poor condition of the track, a
major proportion of the alarmingly high number of derailments
- almost one a day on the system - is attributable to track
defects. The VN was blocked due to derailments for 25% of
the time during 1987, severely affecting capacity.
(ii)  the poor availability and reliability of locomotives, many of
which are due to be scrapped. Their condition, with large
arrears of deferred maintenance, has led tc the mean interval
between line failures falling to as low as 2200 km in 1987
from about 6000 km in 1984, further reducing capacity. SNCZ
has been resorting to hiring diesel locos from the South
African Railways system to tide over severe capacity
problems. The holding of South African Railways wagons has
also been consistently high. These costly arrangements
constitute a drain on foreign exchange (Chapter VII).
(iii) poor availability of rolling stock, with a large back-log of
deferred maintenance; and
(iv)  an inefficient and unreliable telecommunications system, in
need of rehabilitation and modernization.
D. Operations and Efficiency
4.17     While the availability of electric locomotives has improved over
the past years, diesel locomotive availability has been consistently low.
Staff productivity (in terms of traffic units per employee per year)
improved in 1985 and 1986, but fell in 1987, owing to the drop in freight
traffic.

4.18     While the run-down condition of equipment has adverse effects on
operations, management's role in optimizing asset utilization also needs to be
more effective. Current transit time for trains on the VN between
Lubumbashi/Kolwezi and Ilebo is over 15 days, of which nearly 8 days are
excessive yard detentions en route. In order to improve operations management
and control performance, SNCZ, with the assistance of its consultants has
prepared an Operational Action Plan (Annex 1). This plan aims to reduce
transit time from Shaba to Ilebo from 15 to 7.7 days by 1992. During
negotiations, assurance was obtained that the Action Plan will be finalized and
sent to the Association under a Letter of Policy jointly by the Presidents of
SNCZ and ONATRA, committing the two agencies to implement it during the project
life (para. ?.23). This document was received early in October 1988 and this
met one of the conditions of Board Presentation (para. 8.01). The
implementation of this Action Plan in accordance with an agreed timetable is a
condition of the Credit and Project Agreements (para. 8.03).
4.19     Wagon and locomotive utilization needs to be improved.  Initial steps
have already been taken with block trains for export mineral traffic, and this
principle will be extended further in the proposed project. Faster and more
reliable services, coupled with an effective marketing policy is expected to
increase loads in the return direction (imports).



- 34 -
E. Staff end Trainint
4.20     SNCZ has about 22,000 parmanent and 1,800 temporary employees.  Of
the permanent staff, 482 are unskilled, 401 semi-skilled/skilled; 91
supervisory, and 31 managerial. The age profile in the supervisory/
managerial levels is satisfactory, but at the worker level, the average age
is relatively high (441 are over 45). There is a dearth of supervisors,
and positions have been temporarily filled by skilled workers.  Thirty-six
expatriates have direct contracts with SNCZ while 49 are funded under
bilateral and multilateral technical assistance. SNCZ wages are higher
than in the governmental sector, but the basic salary represents only 301
of the total, the balance being made up with various allowances.
Nevertheless, the salary scales need readjustment to reward more fairly
staff with high technical responsibility such as locomotive drivers.
4.21     Personnel management needs to be streamlined and strengthened.
Training is being supervised by a Central Manpower Committee, and
Departmental Training Committees, but coordination between personnel
management and training activities is weak. There are four training
centers which trained 1,300 workers for 4,791 man-months in 1987. In the
supervisory/managerial cadres, 360 staff were trained in 1987. Training
staff suffer from lack of incentives. Technical Assistance in formulating
and implementing an effective personnel and training policy as well as
scholarships for overseas training will be provided by bilateral aid (Annex
3). SNCZ's plan for development of human resources and training was
agreed upon during negotiations (para. 8.03).
4.22     SNCZ's plans to reduce current staff levels to 18,800 by 1992.
Under the Project SNCZ intends to accelerate staff retirement. During
negotiations, agreement was reached for the release by IDA of up to US$1.5
million in support of a retirement fund to be established for the reduction
of some 2,000 employees during 1989 (para. 8.03). In addition, during
negotiations, SNCZ submitted to IDA as part of its manpower planning
policy, its draft technical assistance program for 1989-1992. This was
finalized during negotiations in agreement with IDA and Government (para.
8.03).
F. Tariffs and Costs
4.23     SNCZ is adjusting its tariffs but the financial benefits of such
adjustment have yet to be seen. SNCZ is highly dependent on revenues from
mineral traffic, whose tariffs are indexed. Mineral traffic now brings the
larger part of the company's railway operating revenues (See Chart below).
Though copper, concentrates and other minerals represent half of SNCZ
railway traffic, they account for two thirds of railway operating income.
For general cargo, agricultural produce and passengers, for which tariffs
have not yet been indexed, the share of revenues are much less than their
share of traffic volume. Such cross-subsidy has increased at a level that
is no longer bearable as paying commodities will be with time diverted to
other forms of transport. It is urgent that tariffs be set to reflect more
closely operating costs. During negotiations, Government agreed with the
Association that the Contrat-Proaramme being prepared will aim at cost
based tariffs and will vest the tariff setting responsibility in SNCZ
(para. 4.03, 5.04 and 8.04).

4.24     With the continuous devaluation of the zaire, the revenues in real
terms of a public enterprise such as SNCZ are fluctuating widely.
Coasiderable changes in unit tariffs has taken place in the last few years
(Table 3.01). For example, the passenger fare which was 330Z in 1982
dropped to 140 in 1987 (in constant prices), while the mineral export
tariff, which was 199Z in 1982 increased to 377Z in 1987 also in constant
terms. SNCZ agrees on the need to define and implement new tariff rules,
allowing the company to collect more revenues. During negotiations,
agreement was reached with Government that the Contrat-Programme (para.
4.03) will include provisions authorizing SNCZ to adjust its tariffs (para.
8.04).
4.25     Costs at SNCZ are also increasing in real terms.  The unit cost
(in constant zaires), which was 3.7 zaires/traffic unit in 1980, was 7.7
zaires/traffic unit in i986 (vs. 4.1 on the CFMK railway). Costs fluctuate
wildly due to the incidence of devaluations and other external measures
such as Government-decreed salary increases, and inconsistencies in
accounting procedures. Comparisons even in constant terms do not allow to
draw meaningful conclusions. The technical assistance provided under the
ongoing Second Railway Project is intended to produce detailed and reliable
costing and accounting information.
G. Financial ManaRement
Accounts and Audits
4.26     SNCZ has suffered for many years frow. weak financial management;
procedures and controls are cumbersome and are not observed, information
produced has often been incomplete, unreliable and out of date. This is
borne out by successive external audit reports which are not only heavily
qualified but highly critical of internal control and financial reporting
systems; to make matters worse, weaknesses pointed out by the auditors have
gone largely unheeded. The cost of these deficiencies to SNCZ in lost
revenues and excessive expenditures cannot be quantified but must be
considerable. The deficiencies of the financial reporting system naturally
extend to the cost accounting and budgeting system.



- 36 -
Cost and Management Accounting
4.27     The overall laxism in financial management is evidently not
confined to the general accounting function, but extends to ancillary
services such as budgeting and internal audit. The absence of a reliable
and detailed costing system precludes the establishment of accurate budgets
based on levels or centers of activity; budgets therefore tend to be
"guestimates" extrapolated from latest known levels of revenue and
expenditures. In addition the delay in the production of data means that
by the time it is processed data is often too out of date to be useful; the
need for timely and reliable financial information is acute during periods
of high inflation such as that which Zaire is currently experiencing. The
internal audit department seems totally lacking in motivation and
initiative as well as in modern auditing techniques.
4.28     These weaknesses are principally the result of SNCZ's endemic
problems of under-qualified and poorly-motivated staff, but also stem from
the heavily centralized management structure and its reliance on an
outdated Electronic Data Processing (EDP) system. The proposed project
includes financing for new office technology.
Reforms
4.29     Improvement, however, is under way; these weaknesses were
recognized during previous Bank missions and the ongoing Second Railway
Project (Cr. 1475-ZR) includes consultants' services and technical
assistance to assist SNCZ in, inter alia, a revision of accounting,
budgeting and internal auditing procedures. The consultants, who hold in-
line positions, commenced in 1987 and there are already signs of
.mprovement as a result of their assistance; detailed financial projections
were prepared in conjunction with appraisal of the proposed project and
comprehensive financial accounts and supporting notes for 1987 were
received by IDA within four months of the end of the financial year.
4.30     The other major improvement to financial management will be the
recruitment (presently under way) of an experienced financial expert to
take the position of finance controller and train and assist the Zairean
finance director. The successful candidate will be responsible for
introducing the new accounting and internal control systems being designed
by the consultants and for assuring that they bring about a sustainable
improvement to SNCZ's financial management. He will also be responsible
for ensuring adequate staffing of all departments concerned, for the
training of existing staff as necessary and in particular for that of his
Zairean counterpart.
4.31     The vastness of SNCZ's network and the remoteness of some of its
outlying services are not naturally conducive to the efficient functioning
of a centralized management system and the absence of a reliable
communications network in Zaire exacerbates this problem. The new
financial management systems being designed by the consultants will be
based on the devolition of responsibility for day-to-day management to
regionalized operating units which are small enough to permit information
and management control systems to function within acceptable norms and to
be regularly and easily monitored by management. Regions will be
responsible for their own accounting and internal control systems. The new



- 37 -
systems will be supported by strengthened ex-post controls exercised by
general management (internal audit, budget monitoring, etc.) and the
decentralization of financial management will be implemented on a gradual
basis by region and by level of responsibility. The whole operation is
expected to take several years to complete and the proposed project
includes continued technical assistance to ensure the austainability and
enhance the reliability of the new systems. SNCZ and Government agreed, at
negotiations, that a decentralization plan will be prepared by June 30,
1989 and that implementation will commence before January 1, 1990 (para.
8.03).
H. Plannint and Investment
4.32     The planning function at SNCZ is weak.  A unit has been located as
part of the former PDG's Advisory Office but is understaffed, and SNCZ
relies on consultants to study future investment needs. The new PDG
appointed in July 1988 has proposed some substantial strengthening in the
organization structure. In particular a new Planning and Development
Direction is about to be set up. The proposed project represents 86% of
SNCZ's 1988-92 investment program, the balance being other priority
investments for lake and road transport (Table 4.02). SNCZ intends to
restore (i) its track capacity by renewing large sections of the
infrastructure and (ii) its locomotive and wagon availability by
undertaking the rehabilitation of a part of the fleet and by improving
maintenance. The SNCZ investment plan (1988-92) exceeds by one year the
Public Investment Program (PIP) spanning over the years 1989-91. The Plan
which was submitted in March 1988 at the Paris Donor's meeting for the
transport sector, had been appraised by the mission and found sound,
although some slippage may occur in its implementation, easing the
financial burden over a few more years. The detailed SNCZ's investment and
financing plans for 1989-1992 were finalized in June 1988 (Table 4.03) and
show separately the project components and investments outside the project.
During negotiations, it was agreed with Government and SNCZ (1) that any
change of over $1 million to the investment plan should be subject to IDA's
approval (para. 5.12), (2) that any new investment proposals will be
subject to the annual Public Expenditure Program (PEP) reviews and (3) that
disbursements within any year will be limited to the yearly agreed PEP
figures (para. 8.03).



- 38-
V. THE PROJECT
A. Obiectives
5.01     The proposed project would support Government's objectives and
strategy for improving the efficiency of the transport sector, particularly
along the VN. It would assist in: (i) redefining the role that ONATRA and
SNCZ ought to play in the sector and the contribution of the State therein;
(ii) introducing the institutional changes required in order to fulfill
their roles; and (iii) implementing the physical and financial
rehabilitation required to carry out the agreed upon functions, including
reducing transit time on the VN.
5.02     The project's main objective for ONATRA is institutional reform.
The physical investments proposed are a minimum necessary to provide the
required service levels in areas in which ONATRA will remain active. More
specifically, the project's components for ONATRA will support:
(M)      a major reorganization based on a reorientatP*.; of ONATRA's goals
and strategic role in the transport sector; it will decentralize
and streamline its transport activities through divestiture
and/or reduction of its non-transport activities; further a
'Cor.rat-Proarammen will clarify its relationship with the State;
(ii)     an improvement in its financial performance by restructuring its
debt and by other measures to control its costs and improve its
earntng capacity;
(iii)    an improvemenc in its operating efficiency by rehabilitating
infrastructure, replacing obsolete equipment, improving
management, organizing and facilitating cargo traffic and
dispatch, developing human resources, and improving maintenance of
equipment and infrastructure.
5.03     The main project objective for SNCZ is to restore capacity and
improve operational reliability on the VN. Other objectives are aimed at
improving operations and finances. More specifically, the project's
components for SNCZ will supports
(i)      improvement in operational efficiency and reduction of the
finav-cial deficit;
(ii)     improvement in its operations along the VN by a large program to
rehabilitate track and rolling stock, equipment and
infrastructure; and
(iii)    zlarification of its relationship with the State through a
'Contrat-Programme".



- 39-
B. Proiect Description
5.04     The project covers the period 1989-93 and includes two components
as follows:
I. ONATRA Component
Institutional Measures to: (Chapter III)
(i) Streamline and decentralize ONATRA's organization;
(ii)  Reduce gradually ONATRA's loss-making activities by:
(a) divesting and/or reducing the extent of non-transport
activities (agriculture, forestry, "regie des travaux0,
health, police);
(b) privatizing the shipyard operations;
(c) chartering barges to private companies to take over
selected river transport operations;
(iii) Facilitate the transfer to the private sector of activities
for which ONATRA is ill-suited while improving its efficiency
in carrying out activities (e.g., opening up its main ports
to private operators);
(iv) Establish a container handling and management comnany to
develop inland containerization and manage container yards at
Kinshasa, Ilebo and Kolwesi; and
(v)  Strengthen ONATRA's management and technical cadre through a
carefully designed technical assistance and human resources
development program.
Financial and Operational Measures to:
(i)  Restructure ONATRA's capital and reduce its long-term debt
through the disposal of non-essential assets and a capital
injection by the Government;
(ii) Improve financial management through the delegation of
authority for day-to-day management to the operating units;
and the establishment of appropriate accounting systems
supported by comprehensive and efficient internal controls;
(iii) Continue to implement a short-term financial recovery plan
prepared by ONATRA to reduce staffing levels; improve
management of consumable stores and supplies; improve
collection of revenues;
(iv) Implement an Operational Action Plan to raise productivity on
the Voie Nationale; and



- 40-
(v)  Introduce and implement a 'Contrat-Progrsamme  that would
clearly define the responsibilities and obligations of the
State and ONATRA. These obligations would require monetary
outlays from the public treasury, mainly to pay for the
provision of social transport services. For commercial
transport services, the tariff setting responsibility will be
vested in ONATRA.
Investments: 1
(i)  Matadi Ports  Rehabilitation of two piers; addition of a new
maintenance shop; rehabilitation of quay side cranes; small
craft dolphins; relaying of railway tracks; cargo handling
equipment and parts; and maintenance and shop equipment;
(ii) Kinshasa Port: Repairs to 'Kinsangani" quay; completion of
st3rm drains; new maintenance shop; rehabilitation of quay
side cranes, cargo handling equipment and parts;
(iii) River Navigation and Shipyard: Procurement of rew pushers
and cargo and passenger barges; works and equipment at Ndolo
Shipyard;
(iv) CFMK Railway: minor works at locomotive maintenance
facilities, wagon conversion pits, locomotive shop
maintenance equipment; and track maintenance equipment;
(v)  Containerization of VN:  acquisition of containers and
setting of organizational structure;
(vi) Information Resources: development of Management Information
System with Master Plan, hardware, software, and new
telephone standard; and
(vii) Technical Assistance: 67 man-years of consultant services
for studies, project preparation, supervision of works,
training, etc.
(viii) Human Resources: 23 man-years of consultant services for
strengthening of personnel management, staff training and
establishing a staff fund for accelerated retirement of 2,000
employees in 1989.
II. SNCZ Component
Financial and Operational Measures to:
(i) continue to put into effect short-term measures of the
financial recovery plan prepared by SNCZ including cost
saving measures and staff recuction;
/I A detailed description of proposed investments is given in Annex 4.



- 41-
(ii) prepare a medium-term plan partly based on the findings of
the ongoing studies on uneconomic lines, for streamlining and
decentralizing SNCZ operations and divesting and or reducing
the scope of non-transport activities (health, police); and
(iii)   implement an Operational Act'on Plan to raise productivity on
the Voie Nationale; and
(iv)   introduce and implement a "Contrat-Programme" that would
clearly define the responsibilities and obligations of the
State and SNCZ. These obligations would require monetary
outlays from the public treasury, mainly to pay for the
provision of social transport services. For commercial
transport services, the tariff setting responsibility will be
vested in SNCZ.
Investments:
M()    Tracks  renewal of 303 kms of the most critical sections of
the Voie Nationale, including supply of rails, sleepers,
points and crossings, and track fastenings;
(ii)    Locomotives:  acquisition of 7 mainline and 5 shunting
locomotives to replace scrapped units;
(iii)    Spares: spare parts for rehabilitation, overhaul and
maintenance of locomotives and wagons;
(iv)    Equipment:  acquisition of rrack maintenance machinery,
equipment and tools to replace scrapped units and for
modernization;
(v)    Telecommunicationsp rehabilitation and modernization of the
telecommunications systems;
(vi)    Information Resources:  acquisition of hardware and software
to provide a reliable and effective information system;
(vii)    Technical Assistance:  34 man-years of consultant services
for management support, technical assistance, works
supervision and studies for operational decentralization and
divestiture of some activities to the private sector;
(viii)    Human Resources:  14 man-years (to be funded outside the
Projeat) of consultant services for strengthening of
personnel management, man-power planning and development,
training of staff, and establishing a staff fund for
accelerated retirement of 2,000 employees in 1989.
C. Cost Estimates and Financing
5.05     The total cost of the proposed project is US$280.1 million,
US$103.2 million for ONATRA and US$176.9 million for SNCZ. Foreign costs
amount to US$243.4 million. Costs are summarized in Table 5.01(T) and



- 42-
detailed estimates in Table 5.01. Cost estimates for ONATRA and SNCL
components have been prepared jointly by their staff and engineering
consultants, financed by TDA under ongoing credits, reviewed by the
appraisal mission, and revised as appropriate to reflect known costs for
similar items at December 31, 1987 prices.  Local taxes are not included in
the estimates as both enterprises have been exempted therefrom under
previous and ongoing projects. Physical contingencies have been
calculated at 15% of base costs for civil works, 10% for equipment, and 0%
for consultants and training.  Price escalation for foreign and local costs
is based on projected increases in international prices in US$ of 3.2% p.a.
in 1988, 1989 and 1990, and 0.8% p.a. thereafter (Bank projections as of
February 17, 1988). International rates have also been used for local cost
escalation assuming that the difference between domestic and international
price inflation will be offset by adjustments in the foreign exchange rate,
in accordance with Government policy.
5.06     Cost estimates have been derived as follows: (i) for equipment,
spare parts, materials and supplies, defined in most cases at final
specification level, from engineer's estimates supported by informal
quotations; this is acceptable as most project items are standard "off-the-
shelf" components; (ii) for civil works, where applicable, from
preliminary engineering estimates for quantities and on current contracts
for the unit prices; (iii) for technical assistance and engineering
services, from actual costs for similar services rendered under other
projects in Zaire. Cost estimates were reviewed and agreed upon between
the Government, ONATRA and SNCZ, and the Association during negotiations
(paras. 8.02 and 8.03).
5.07     A financing plan for ONATRA and SNCZ was proposed at a donors
meeting held in Paris in March 1988 and finalized at negotiations (Table
5.02 T). The project would be financed as follows (in US$ millions): IDA
75.0, AfDB 91.6, KfW 15.0, EEC 6.2, Belgium 24.1, CCCE 36.2, ONATRA 15.2,
and SNCZ 16.8. 90% of the total project cost is expected to be financed by
foreign cofinanciers. IDA's share of total cost will be 27%, representing
32% of ONATRA's component (Table 5.02) and 24% of SNCZ's (Table 5.03).
During negotiations, it was agreed that loan effectiveness will be declaied
when agreements have been signed with the financiers supporting the track
renewal program, the implementation of which is on the critical path (para.
8.04).

D. Project Implementation
ONATRA
5.08     The executing agency will be ONATRA itself, with each deportment
in charge of its own components; ONATRA has demonstrated it can execute
projects reasonably well; the two ongoing projects - credits 1335-ZR and
1180-ZR are nearly complete. The office of the technical secretary,
attached to the general manager, will carry out overall project
coordination, reinforced by technical assistance. Technical assistance to
management and to the various operating departments, provided either by the
project itself, or by bilateral assistance will ensure the project
implementation quality level.
5.09     Civil works at Matadi and some critical specific tasks in other
departments will require supervision by consultants. Project preparation
is well-advanced- most studies are complete and most bid documents are
being prepared by ONATRA. Preparation of civil works bid documents will be
funded out of the credit proceeds. Procurement will begin early 1989; most
of the project is expected to be completed by 1992 ard the entire project
by 1994. The implementation schedules, shown in Chart 7 and Annex 1
(Attachment 2) were agreed upon during negotiations (para. 8.02).
SNCZ
5.10     SNCZ will act as its own executing agency through its various
departments. Though the Second Railway Project (Credit 1475-ZR)
experienced start-up delays, management has been strengthened by technical
assistance, in joint executive capacity. The project is now progressing
well and a similar arrangement would be used for implementing the proposed
project. Overall project coordination would be the responsibility of
general management. Supervision of civil works and telecommunications will
oe carried out by SNCZ, assisted by independent consultants funded by the
project. Project start-up and completion are expected to be similar to
ONATRA's. The implementation schedules shown in Chart 8 and Annex 1
(Attachment 3), were agreed upon during negotiations (para. 8.03).

The project has been divided into specific lots which have been allocated
to the various financing sources, thus permitting parallel financing under
separate procurement rules.   The following items (Table 5.03(T)) will be
procured by ICB under the IDA credit: (i) most civil works at Matadi; (ii)
port equipment; (iii) wagon modernization parts; (iv) conteiners for
ONATRA; and (v) rails; and (vi) track works for SNCZ. Minor works at
Matadi and Kinshasa will be procured by Local Competitive Bidding under the
IDA credit. Proprietary spare parts available only from original or from
limited number of suppliers may be procured by direct or International
Shopping, subject to the limitations described below. Consultants'
services financed by the credit will be procured in accordance with IDA
guidelines. SONATRAD, the Government purchasing agency set up in 1985 to
centralize procurement for five public enterprises, will carry out all
procurement of goods commonly used by ONATRA, SNCZ and other public
enterprises. For specific items not commonly used, SONATRAD intervenes
only above US$3.0 million equivalent. Recent experience with SONATRAD has
been satisfactory after a somewhat slow start. During negotiations it was
agreed that on-going agreements between ONATRA and SNCZ on one side, and
SONATRAD on the other, will not be modified without IDA's prior agreement
(para. 8.02 and 8.03).
5.12     The cumulative value of items procured by local competitive
bidding and funded by IDA will not exceed US$1 million for ONATRA and US$1
million for SNCZ. International shopping can be used for items not
exceeding US$100,000 individually, and US$2.5 million cumulative value
separately for ONATRA. All procurement decisions exceeding US$1 million
and not included in the Project Description, Anner 4, require approval by
the Association independently of their source of :inancing; this was agreed
with both enterprises during negotiations. All bid documents for items to
be financed by IDA estimated to cost more than US$100,000 will require
previous review and approval by IDA. It is estimated that over 90% of the
proceeds of the credit will require previous review by IDA. These rules
were agreed to during negotiations (paras. 8.02 and 8.03)).
5.13     Local preferences in accordance with IDA guidelines will be
available to qualified domestic bidders for ICB contracts. ONATRA and SNCZ
are exempted from customs duties in IDA financed project components
supplied from abroad. LCB procedures utilized by ONATRA and SNCZ in
previous projects have been found generally satisfactory.
F. Disbursements
5.14     The IDA credit would be disbursed on the following basis:
(i) Civil Works: 80% of total costs for ONATRA component, and
100% of track renewal contract for SNCZ component, based on
justification by SNCZ that it has apent its share of own
account works valued at about 20% of the track renewal
program;
(ii)  Snare parts, supplies, materials and quipment: 100% of
foreign expenditures; 80% of local expenditures for ex-
factory costs and 75% of local expenditures for other items
procured locally;
(iii) Technical assistance, consulting services and data
processing: 100% of foreign expenditures; and



- 47-
(iv) Training fellowship, teaching materials and severance
payments: 100% of total expenditures.
(v) Early Retirement Program: 50% of total expenditures for the
provision of training for slternative outside employment or
of capital goods to help retirees to start such outside
employment. No disbursement will be made against any cash
payments to employees or retirees.
5.15     All claims would be fully documented except for those relating to
any contract, purchase order, or training activity whose value is less than
$20,000 and which would be submitted under statements of expenditure. The
documentation for these claims would be held by ONATRA and SNCZ and made
available to IDA supervision missions for inspection. In order to
facilitate and expedite disbursements, ONATRA and SNCZ will each open a
Special Account in a foreign currency and in a commercial bank on terms and
conditions acceptable to the Association. The Association will deposit an
amount of US$0.5 million equivalent in foreign exchange in each Special
Account. The accounts would be replenished on the basis of withdrawal
applications submitted monthly. These applications will also comprise a
statement of account movements since the last application and a
reconciliation showing that the balance represents the original amount
deposited in the account less payments for which a withdrawal application
is submitted. In addition, $400,000 will be added to the authorized amount
of each Special Account to finance 3 months of accelerated retirement of
2000 staff (paras. 3.06 and 5.04). The Special Accounts will be
replenished at intervals as needed, and after documented statements of
disbursements have been submitted to IDA. All these arrangements were
agreed to at negotiations (para. 8.05).
5.16     The estimated schedule of disbursements of IDA funds for both
ONATRA and SNCZ combined is shown on Table 5.04. The disbusement schedule
follows the typical disbursement profile for Zaire. The closing date is
December 31, 1995.
G. Environmental Impact
5.17     None of the project components represents additions to ONATRA and
SNCZ's network, or supply of services to new geographic areas. The
proposed actions and components consist of equipment replacement, civil
works rehabilitation, and institutional measures. Thus, the project is not
expected to have any environmental impact. The establishment of a Harbour
Master office in Kinshasa will decrease garbage discharge into the river
and thus improve the environment.
H. Prolect Evaluation and Monitorint
5.18     ONATRA and SNCZ are expected to continue with the same operating
efficiency indicators used for monitoring previous IDA credits (Tables 2.02
to 2.05 (T)). In addition to the above, and in order to monitor better the
institutional strength of ONATRA, a number of new performance indicator"
have been defined jointly and included in Annex 1, with target figures
through 1995. They were reviewed at negotiations; ONATRA and SNCZ
respectively confirmed that the present system of reporting including
completion reports, for the Matadi-Kinshasa Port Rehabilitation Project and
Second SNCZ Project respectively will be continued for monitoring the First
Transport Rehabilitation Project, (paras. 8.02 and 8.03).

VI.  ECONOMIC EVALUATION
A. Traffic Forecasts
6.01      Future traffic on the VN sytem will be influenced by:
(M)     growth of Zaire's economy and population;
(ii)    production levels of main exports or locally-consumed
items, such as copper, zinc, palm-oil, coffee, rubber,
logs, maize, etc.;
(iii)   relative competivity of the VN, vs. other routes;
(iv)    ability of the state-owned enterprises to maintain or
recover a large part of the traffic.
To minimize the uncertainty of these elements, consultants' studies have
considered separately each of the main commodities (Table 6.01), making
assumptions on their market, their traffic share between parastatal and
private sectors and their potential for containerization. Traffic
forecasts along the whole Voie Nationale system, summarized below, are
shown in Table 6.02 to 6.07.

6.02      The above freight flow estimates on various route segments reflect
low rates of growth in the range of 1 to 3Z per year.  Prospects for
anything other than moderate increases in transport of principal
commodities are not bright:



- 49 -
(i)     copper and other mineral exports are expected to increase
at only 0.6% per year. Gecamines, which is the largest
mining company, predicts stable production at current
levels (547,000 tons a year) until 1990, and a small
increase up to 575,000 tons by 1995. Production will
either stabilize at the 1995 level, or increase to 645,000
tons by 2000 (justified by the opening of a new zinc
operation in Kipushi).  In other respects, a growing part
of minerals will be transported by road for long haul, as
new contracts are opened with road haulers;
(ii)    imports on the Kinshasa-Shaba axis are closely linked to
minerral production and should slowly increase;
(iii)   traffic of agricultural products for local consumption is
unlikely to increase.  First, transport represents a large
portion of the total food cost in consumption areas, and
the low buying power of consumers limits demand (for
instance, price of maize in Kinshasa is respectively 2.8
and 4.2 times higher than in Kikwit and in Ilebo
production areas). Second, while the distributitin of
imported food is well organized, local productior. uses
traditional means that are not efficient and often
discouraging (maize planters have sometimes to wait so
long for a river barge that their crops are lost). Third,
imported food like flour has often replaced local food in
dietary habits, and this trend will unfortunately be
difficult to reverse.
6.03     Some freight is, however, expected to grow, parLicularly:
Mi)     imported food, as a result of previous considerations and
the little success of the food self sufficiency policy;
(ii)    logs, whose potential traffic could be large if adequate
river and rail transport capacity were available.
Commercial development of this traffic by ONATRA could
lead to its doubling by 1995;
(iii)   general cargo (vehicles, equipment, containers), essential
for the functioning of the Zairean economy (and
particularly the mining industry);
6.04     It is also assumed that the state-owned transport companies will
not be able to take advantage of some other developing traffic, like coffee
or cocoa.  Locally called "produits de convoitise" (coveted goods), these
high value-added products will probably be transported by private
operators, because of the risk of theft and waste for perishables. In any
case, volumes are relatively small.
6.05     Container traffic will continue to grow in Matadi, but at a slower
pace than during recent years, depending on the development of
containerized copper shipments. Chart 2 provides information on container



- 50 -
transport volume in Zaire in 1986. Since 1980, container traffic has
increased 11% a year in the Matadi port, reaching 471,000 tons in 1986 but
it is likely to stabilize in the future as most general cargo and non-
mineral traffic is already containerized. However, copper containerization
should grow because of the ease of handling in intermodal transhipment.
The rate of containerization, which is currently about 30% in Matadi port,
should reach 36Z by 2000.
6.06     On the VN, development of containerization is highly dependent on
the way it is organized. Kinshasa port, which handled about 7%,000 tons of
containers in 1980 now handles less than 70,000 tons, because of the
Kinshasa Terminal Container (TCK) competition. Road transport is
generally prefered to CFMK for containers unloaded in Matadi, because of
better service and customs practices. The establishment of a semi-private
company for handling and dispatching containers along the VN, would restore
reliability and customer confidence and allow for a significant increase in
traffic. This proposal will be pursued under the project which will
provide funds on investment in containers.
B. Operations with and without Proiect
6.07     The decline in public transport capacity and traffic in Zaire has
been due mainly to lack of maintenance and replacement of equipment and
facilities, and also to managerial and marketing insufficiencies which have
weakened ONATRA and SNCZ. As a consequence, a major rehabilitation program
in both parastatals is now needed, including physical investments and
institutional improvements, and enmhasising human factors (organization,
incentives, training, technical assistance). The proposed project is
intended to arrost the decline of the public transport sector which market
would grow at 3X a year over the next few years.
6.08     The economic evaluation of the project is based on a comparison of
alternative scenarios with and without the project. The scenario with the
project is described in Chapter V and in Annex 1 presenting an Operational
Action Plan on the VN system. The scenario without the project, also
called "reference scenario', can be defined as follows:
(i)     without the project, ONATRA and SNCZ would continue to
operate their infrastructure and fleet by spending limited
but growing amounts of money and time on maintenance.
Despite this, the availability of locos, wagon, pushers,
barges, cranes would slightly decline every year. The
number of derailments and traffic interruptions would
increase. The delays and immobilizations suffered by both
equipment and transported goods would also increase;
(ii)    such an inefficient operation would not significantly
reduce the traffic handled by ONATRA and SNCZ over the
short to medium term. This apparently paradoxical
assumption is justified by the fact that except for Matadi
Port, there is no capacity limitation along the VN system.
In the refereLre scenario almost the same traffic could be
carried by the parastatals as in the "with project'



- 51 -
alternative, the major difference being lower efficiency
and higher operating costs. The recurrence of problems
and lower quality of service by ONATRA and SNCZ would
discourage their clients, but demand is rather inelastic,
since current traffic which represents the core of
parastatal's market (minerals, heavy goods, low-value
freight) is captive and would not decr ase much more;
(iii)   under the reference scenario, no changes are foreseen in
traffic shares by routes. Although for purely financial
reasons the mining traffic could divert to other routes,
this is unlikely for political reasons. Zaire has a
legitimate interest in maintaining domestic capacity for
the transport of sizeable volumes of its principal
exports. The opening of the Lobito route is not
foreseeable in the near future and has therefore been
disregarded in the scenarios.
C. Proiect Benefits and Economic Return
6.09     The main benefits of the proposed project would be derived from
the continuing modernization cf ONATRA and SNCZ, allowing:
Mi)     these companies to improve their operating efficiency
(speed increase, better use of equipment, improved
availability, etc.) and to reduce their costs (cheaper
maintenance, fuel or personnel savings, etc.);
(ii)    the Zairean (or foreign) community to take advantage of
sustained low tariffs, of speedier shipments (reduction of
Iimobilizations, better reliability, etc.), and of savings
obtained comparatively to alternative investments.
Details are given in Annex 5 on the economic evaluation for ONATRA's
investments, respectively for Matadi and Kinshasa ports, CFMK railway, and
river transport and shipyard, and for SNCZ's investments including track
renewal, mainline and shunting locomotives and telecommunications. The
streams of costs and benefits over the economic life of the project are
shown in Table 6.08.
6.10     The project is expected to allow a decrease in operating costs
along the VN, giving this route a new competitiveness vs. the other routes.
Table 6.02 (T) below shows that the unit costs should decrease by about 10%
on ONATRA/SNCZ system during the 1988-1995 period, despite the effects of
high investment levels on depreciation (especially in SNCZ) and the low
growt.h of traffic. The figures of this table (details are given in Table
6.09) have been computed with the charges resulting from the financial
simulation presented in Chapter VII (Tables 7.01 to 7.08).

6.11     The combined effects of more competitive costs (about the same in
1995 as those of Eastern or Southern routes) and higher speed (21 days from
Shaba to Matadi, vs. 36 days on the best current route) will boost the
development of the VN. This could result in new traffics (development of
local traffic, recovering of Gicamines supplies, diversion of some Zambian
exports, etc.), which have not been taken into consideration in the
economic and financial forecasts, focused on the 1988-1995 period, but are
definitely a major trump for the future.
D. Overall Fczonomic Evaluation and Risks
6.12     The internal rate of return (IRR) of the entire project is estimated
to be about 14%, which is acceptable in view of the very conservative traffic
assumptions taken into account. The IRR of the major components range from
11 to 18%, except for river transport investment, whose profitability is low,
but which is absolutely necessary to ensure reliability along the Voie
Nationale (cargo barges) and to encourage local supply and integration with
the hinterland (passengers barges). In recent years, ONATRA has considerably
cut its services on Zaire River (convoys to and from Kisangani), and poor
navigability on the Kasai river has often delayed copper exports. The
various IRR of the project's components, are summarized belows

6.13     The main risks of not reaching the expected benefit levels are
linked to:
(i)    managerial or institutional weaknesses of ONATRA and SNCZ.
The project assumes that both parastatals will address these
problems in an adequate way, so that a substantial recovery
in efficiency and traffic is achieved (Chapter III);
(ii)   possible traffic diversion to other routes.  Gecamines is
already using either truck operation (on Kinshasa-Matadi or
Shaba-Dar Es Salaam, for exports; on the southern road, for
supply or southern railways.  As a counterpart to investments
under the proposed project that will improve the transit
efficiency, SN(Z will negotiate with the Zairean mining
industry a garanteed minimum traffic on the VN for at least
5 years.
(iii)  possible increase in project costs.
6.14     Though the economic analysis uses realistic cost and traffic
assumptions, a sensitivity analysis was carried out to test variotis changes,
On the basis respectively of a 102 and 201 increase in project cost, the
resulting IRR would decrease to 12 and 101 but would still be satisfactory.
On the basis of a traffic change of + 10, the resulting returns would also
be satisfactory as shown below. This suggests that the project has been
designed in a conservative way. Indeed, the priority of renewing or
replacing items under the project would be still substantial even if the
current declining traffic trend in the public transport sector goes on.

VII. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
I. ONATRA
A. Past Performance and Present Financial Situation
7.01     ONATRA's Income statements, balance sheets and movement of funds
statements for the years 1983 to 1995 are given in Tables 7.01 to 7.04.
The data below illustrate the decline in ONATRA's financial situation since
1984:

This decline .; principally the result of:
(i)     poor financial control by ONATRA and insufficient tariff
increases by Government to compensate for the rapid
inflation; CFMK railway and river transport costs have
respectively risen 501 and 301 faster than tariffs since
1983;
(ii)    an ambitious and poorly-focussed investment program since
1980 with large external financing, which has not been
recouped by increased profitability. The weight of
ONATRA's debt service has been compounded by the massive
devaluations of the zaire.
In addition, ONATRA's centralized administrative structure has
hampered efficient management performance, stifling initiative and
motivation at lower levels and probably contributing to the fall in river
and rail traffic.



- 55 -
7.02     ONATRA's sea ports (notably Matadi) are the exception to the
decline due to increased profitability from their virtual monopoly of trade
and port stevedoring charges invoiced in Deutsch Marks. The lack of
financial transparency between departments precludes meaningful analyses of
costs and revenues, but ports are clearly subsidizing other operations, as
shown below:
Table 7.02(T) Shares of Revenues and Costs
1984      1985       1986      1987
rev.costs rev.costs rev.costs rev.costs

The fall in the head office share of revenue reflects the turnaround in
ONATRA's liquidity; previously the significant cash reserves were invested
by ONATRA's management.
Reform
7.03     At the end of 1987, in an attempt to halt this decline and restore
ONATRA's long-term credibility, the Government appointed a new President
Delegue General (PDG). Under his direction ONATRA has:
(i)     announced a program of tariff increases to restore them to
former levels in relation to costs; the first increases,
averaging 50%, were implemented in February 1988 but
another 30% planned for July 1988 was blocked by
Government; appropriate tariff adjustments were made as of
January 1, 1989;
(ii)    instituted controls to improve productivity and
accountability and ensure a return to operating
profitability in 1988. Measures incorporated in a 'Plan
de Redressement Financier a Court Terme" comprise strict
budgetary and procurement controls, better monitoring of
fuel consumption, suppression of overtime and early
retirement of personnel;
(iii)   revamped its "Projet d'entreprise 1988-1992" and committed
itself to the institutional reforms enshrined therein
(Chapter III). The divestiture or contracting out by
ONATRA of many of its ancillary and costly services should
generate considerable economies;
(iv)    as part of the "Contrat-Programme" to be agreed between
the Government and ONATRA, sought to be granted the
freedom in setting its tariffs.
These measures should significantly reduce the need for central
administration, which currently employs some 4,000 staff (out of a total of
18,300) and accounts for 27% of ONATRA's operating expenses.



- 56 -
7.04     Although these reforms should restore ONATRA's viability in the
long-term, further measures are necessary to redress its existing
situation: ONATRA is unable to support its debt service; arrears to the
national debt-servicing agency OGEDEP (Office de Gestion de la Dette
Publique) amounted to Z3.9 billion (SDRs 21 million) at end-1987. A
restructuring of its debt is essential before any new borrowing may take
place. Table 7.01(T) above shows that without restructuring the debt/equity
ratlo wculd have risen to 60:40 at end-1987, an unacceptably high figure
for ONATRA. At negotiations, the Association received from the Government
a copy of its letter to ONATRA approving capitalizatior. of ONATRA's arrears
to OGEDEP and of part of its long-term debt for a total of Zll billion.
It was agreed that the Presidential Ordinance authorizing the accounting
entries would be sent to the Association as a condition of Project
effectiveness.
B. Financial Proiections
7.05     Assumptions used for the projections are explained in Annex 6.
The principal objective underlying the projections is for ONATRA to
generate sufficient free capital to allow it rt finance a reasonable
portion of its future investments and reduce its long-term debt burden.

Results and comments
7.06     The projections show that the combination of better financial and
personnel management and inflation-linked tariff increases should enable
0iATRA to produce a positive rate of return on equity from 1990, which
should be acceptable to IDA. The jump in profitability from 1992 to 1995
is largely due to the absence in the projections of any investments after
1993. The projections reveal the generation of investment capacity, to
facilitate the planning of future investments and of borrowing
requirements. ONATRA's current investment program of $181 million (Z69
billion at forecast exchange rates) represents the limit of its present
financing capacity; the debt/equity ratio rises to 54:46 in 1993 before it
starts to fall.
Risks
7.07     The main risks attached to these projections are (i) failure of
Government reforms to arrest the tumbling exchange rate and bring inflation
down and (ii) failure of ONATRA to contain cost increases or to redress its
declining shares of traffic. It is assumed that under the proposed
"Contrat-ProarammeH the responsibility for tariff increases will be vested
in ONATRA s management.
