D. Financial Covenants
7.09     At negotiations, Government and ONATRA agreed to take all measures
necessary, including the indexing of tariffs for general freight,
agricultural products and passengers, to ensure that the following specific
indicators of financial performance be met and be included as financial
covenants in the Project Agreement with ONATRA (para. 8.02).
(i)     working expenses will not exceed 68% of operating revenues
for 1989, 62% for 1990 and 60% there%fter;
(ii)    a positive return will be earned on equity (capital
employed less long term debt) from 1991;
(iii)   liquidity ratios (current assets excluding stocks over
current liabilities) will be maintained of at least 1.4
for 1989, 1.7 for 1990 and 2.0 thereafter; and
(iv)    long-term debt will not exceed accumulated equity and
reserves (including revaluation surpl'lses).
In addition, it was agreed that tariff adjustments for 1988 will be enacted
as a condition of Board Presentation of this project (para. 8.01).
Onlendint
7.10     The on-lending terms of anticipated aid from AfDB, Belgian and
West GermAn and CCCE loans are expected to vary between zero and 7.5Z
Interest and 10 to 30 years repayment with grace periods of between 4 and



- 58 -
10 years. ONATRA's share of the proposed IDA credit will be relent to
ONATRA by the Government at current Bank rate at date of Board Presentation
(7.59% as of July 20, 1988) interest over a 20 years repayment period with
5 years' grace. This was agreed to at negotiations. As ONATRA will be
assuming the foreign exchange risk, the interest rate is positive (para.
8.02).
Auditing and Reporting Requirements
7.11     During negotiations, it was agreed that the fA,l1ow'ng financial
documents will be submitted to the Association within the timetable
indicated (para. 8.02):
(i)     Draft (pre-audit) annual financial statements not later
than four months after the end of the year to which they
relate;
(ii)    The interim audit report by 31st December of each year;
(iii)   Audited financial statements not later than six months
after the end of the year to which they relate;
(iv)    Budgets, projected Balance Sheets and cash flow forecasts
with accompanying forecasts for following years by 30th
November preceding the year to which they relate;
(v)     Any other available documents which from time to time the
Association may reasonably require.

7.14     SNCZ's poor operating performance and weak middle and lower-
management have aggravated the situation. Its financial performance has
been particularly affected in recent years by the Zaire devaluation and
high inflation. Nevertheless, compared to many other African railways,
this performance since 1983 appears far from discreditable. Except for
1986, working expenses have not exceeded 80% of revenues (compared with an
average of between 90Z and 100% in the late 1970's) and a positive working
capital and liquidity were maintained until 1987. However, liquidity is
badly short since 1987 which coincided with the massive fall of the Zaire
currency and full-scale implementation of SNCZ's Second Railway project.
Also, past under-investment has led to a reduction in operating capacity;
forcing SNCZ to lease rolling stock and locomotives from South Africa at a
cost of US$4.5 and 7.3 million in 1986 and 1987 respectively.
Reform
7.15     The following steps have or are being taken which should
significantly improve the situation:
(i)     The strengthening of SNCZ's top management: in 1987 a
(Zairean) general manager and (expatriate) assistant
general manager were appointed with IDA's approval; in
August 1988 a new general manager has been nominated who
is planning major improvements in the organization
structure and key staffing with a view to strengthen
accountability;



- 60 -
(ii)    The arrival of two teams of technical assistants, financed
by IDA (Cr.1475-ZR), to strengthen SNCZ's operational and
financial management; most of these experts hold in-line
positions in the company (see Chapter IV);
(iii)   The employment of an expatriate financial controller, also
financed under the existing project and whose recruitment
is presently under way;
(iv)    The indexation of mineral tariffs; in 1987 average mineral
tariffs increased to Z8 per ton/km from Z3.2 per ton/km in
1986, an increase of 150%; other freight tariffs and
passenger tariffs increased by 65% and 41% respectively.
Revenues from mineral traffic rose from 35% of total
revenue in 1983 to 42% in 1987; and
(v)    As part of the "contrat-programme" to be agreed between
the Government and SNCZ, the freedom in setting tariffs is
to be granted to SNCZ's management.
The new management's measures for improving operational performance and
reduce costs are detailed in a "Programme a Court Terme de Redressement
Financier". They focus on fuel consumption, travel expenses and maximum
utilization of SNCZ's own rolling stock to minimize leasing equipment from
overseas in hard currency. The resulting savings are (conservatively)
reflected in the 1988 Budget, which limits the rise in operating costs to
53%, compared to inflation estimated at 65%.
7.16     The cross subsidization between services and regions is presently
difficult to quantify. SNCZ's centralized administration and data
processing systems obscure financial transparency between regions and make
- difficult to identify costs by type of traffic and by region, and to
justify requests by SNCZ for specific subsidies for structurally
unprofitable operations from Government. The ongoing technical assistance
will assist SNCZ in establishing reliable information systems. Also, a
reorganization study will be carried out based on the recommendations of an
ongoing low density lines study (part of which will be implemented without
delay) (para. 4.05). The carrying out of such a study was agreed to during
negotiations (para. 8.03).
B. Financial Proiections
7.17     The principal objective underlying the projections is for SNCZ to
generate sufficient funds to allow it to finance a reasonable portion of
its future investments and maintain its debt burden within its debt
servicing capacity.
Table 7.06(T) Financial Projections: Key Indicators

- 61 -
7.18     The projections, including depreciation on revalued fixed assets,
allow for significant cash generation representing by end 1995 more than a
quarter of net fixed assets, which shoulo be acceptable to IDA. The
projections do not take account of operating subsidies from Government for
structurally unprofitable services, which should be identified during the
life of the project and incorporated in a future contrat-programme.  This
will eliminate the need for the present cross-subsidisation and enable SNCZ
to generate a satisfactory return on equity from its profitable operations.
7.19     The improvements considered in the proposed project include:
(i)     More reliable services and reduced transport times which
will increase capacity and allow SiNCZ to recapture lost
traffic;
(ii)    Better equipment availability, obviating the need to hire
equiprient from overseas;
(iii)   Reiuced track maintenance costs;
(iv)    New information systems to enable management to react
promptly and appropriately to changes and problems.
7.20     The measurea mentioned above are expected to produce:
(i)     positive operating result by 1991 (after loan interest and
after providing for depreciation on revalued fixed
assets);
(ii)    positive liquidity by 1990;
(iii)   positive net result by 1995.
Until the new information systems can accurately identify the cost to SNCZ
of operating structurally non-profitable services aend demand specific
subsidies from Government to cover such costs, it is not realistic to
expect SNCZ to earn a positive rate of return on its capital and reserves.
At negotiations, the Government and SNCZ agreed that SNCZ submit by June
1989 a detailed request for (i) means to cover for structually loss-making
services that SNCZ is required to operate by Government or (ii) permission
to terminate such services within a specified delay (para. 8.03).
Risks
7.21 The major risks to the project are:
(i)     Opening of the Benguela railway line through Angola; due
to the present political situation this is not considered
likely within the foreseeable future;
(ii)    A major cut-back in copper production, or the diversion of
more and more copper exports to other transport routes;
such events would be resisted by Government but might be
beyond its control in case of political disburbances in
the region.



- 62 -

7.22     The two elements to which SNCZ's financial performance is
particularly sensitive are traffic growth and staff reductions. The effect
of traffic and personnel remaining at their forecast 1988 levels would be
as follows:

The proposed component of the project to finance the early retirement of
some 2000 employees should ensure that necessary staff reductions take
place; the major investaents in the proposed project to restore SNCZ's
operating capacity should ensure that much of its natural bulk traffic is
recovere1.
D. Financial Covenants
7.23     At negotiations, Government and SNCZ agreed to take all measures
necessary, including the indexing of tariffs for general freight and first
and second class passenger traffic, to ensure that the following specific
indicators of financial performance be met, and be included as financial
covenants in the project agreement with SNCZ (para. 8.03):
(i)     working expenses not to exceed 75% of operating revenue
for 1989, 70% for 1990, 65% from 1991 and 57% from 1993;
(ii)    liquidity to be not less than 0.8 in 1989 and 1990 and 1.0
thereafter (liquidity being defined as the excess of
current assets excluding stocks over current liabilities);
and
(iii)   a positive return to be earned on equity from 1995 (equity
being defined as all capital and reserves, including long-
term provisions and unappropriated profits, but excluding
long-term debt).
In addition, it was agreed that tariff adjustments for 1988 will be enacted
as a condition of Board Presentation of this project (para. 8.01).



- 63 -
Onlending
7.24     The on-lending terms of anticipated aid from Belgium anid the
Federal Republic of Germany, and from AfDB and CCCE loans, are expected to
vary between zero and 7.5% interest and 10 to 30 years repayment with grace
periods of between 4 and 10 years. SNCZ's share of the proposed IDA credit
will be relent to SNCZ by the Government at current Bank rate at date of
Board Presentation (7.59% as of July 20, 1988) over 20 years' repayment
period with 5 years' grace. As SNCZ will be assuming the foreign exchange
risk, this interest rate is positive (para. 8.03).
Auditing and Reporting Requirements
7.25     During negotiations, it was agreed that the following financial
documents to be submitted to the Association within the timetable indicated
(para. 8.03):
(i)     Draft (pre-audit) annual financial statements not later
than four months after the end of the year to which they
relate;
(ii)    Audited financial statements not later than six months
after the end of the year to which they relate;
(iii)   Audited Budgets, projected Balance Sheets and cash flow
statements with forecasts for the next three years and the
auditors' report thereon, by 30th November preceding the
year to which the:, relate;
(iv)    Any other available documents which the Association may
from time to time reasonably require.

ANNEX 1
ZAIRE
FIRST TRANSPORT REHABILITATION PROJECT tONATRAISNCZ)
Operational Action Plan
1.       The implementation of the physical, institutional, technical
assistance and training components of the project will need to be
supplemented, supported and coordinated with an effective Plan of
Operations with agreed targets of performance. This Plan of Operations
will ensure timely and effective utilisation of the project inputs, and
realisation of the projected benefits. ONATRA and SNCZ have agreed to
produce such a joint coordinated plan, and the submission of such a plan
acceptable to the Association, was a condition for Board presentation.
A. Main Actions
2.       The salient points of ths Plan, as well as important performance
indicators have been agreed upon. The main thrust of the Plan of
Operations would be on the following aspects:
Mi)      Containerisation of copper exports, in order to reduce handling
time and improve operating efficiency.
(ii)     Rationalisation of rail and river operations to improve asset
utilisation and to reduce transit time of cargo. This would be supported
by improved maintenance management, spare parts imports, and institutional
improvements.
(iii)    Enhanced standards of performance monitoring.  Apart from better
internal monitoring of performance within each of the entities - viz Matadi
Porc, CFMK Railway, Kinshasa Port, Voies Fluviales and SNCZ, the
decentralisation measures proposed under the project will call for strict
accountability between systems at interfaces, and the financial adjustments
that would accompany it.
(iv)     Improvements of standards of personnel discipline and performance,
leading to better staff morale and productivity.
(a) Containerization of Cargos
3.       The primary uxport cargo is copper, which is transported in three
forms - blister, cathode sheets, and wire bars.  The blister copper is in
the form of a crude ingot of size approximately 70 cms x 70 cms x 10 cms.
The irregular shape of the blisters makes stackivg difficult. The sheets
have dimensions of 70 cms x 70 cms and are about 0.5 cm thick and they are
bundled into thicknesses of about 40 cms. There is a large incidence of
breakage of bundles. The wire bars are in the form of ingots about a meter
long, and with a width of about 10 cms. These are relatively easier to
stack and bundle, but there is a large incidence of breakage of straps.
Handling of broken bundles calls for time and labor-consuming operations.
These delays are reflected not only in the transit time of the product, but
also in excessive crane-time, and delays to rolling stock and barges, as
well as incidence of thefts and losses.



-69-
4.       Selection and provision of a suitable container to transport these
products from the factory to their final destination in Zaire, if not to
their final destination abroad, would reduce delays all around. Adequate
cranes and other container handling facilities are available at Ilebo,
Kinshasa and Matadi ports.
5.       Currently, a 4 ton crane handles 4 to 8 wagon loads of copper per
shift of eight hours. Containerised copper would need only two hours per
train load. It is estimated that a minimum of two days can be saved in the
transit time of the copper at each of the transhipment points - Ilebo,
Kinshasa and Matadi. Containerisation would also result in reducing the
train loading time by at least 12 hours at Kolwezi and Lubumbashi.
6.       The containerization study envisaged under Cr. 1180-ZR is expected
to produce suitable recommendations on the design, utilization and
maintenance of the containers needed, and on -he system of operations
management and control. In particular, the prDject will support the
procurement of containers and the establishment of a container handling and
management company which would develop and market inland containerization
and manage handling interfaces, and contair.-r yards at Kinshasa, Ilebo,
Kolwezi and possibly Matadi (para. 6.06).
(b) Rationalization of Operations:
(i)      Ports of Matadi and Kinshasa
7.       The system of documentation and clearance would be simplified in
order to streamline it and reduce transit time of cargo.
8.       Private sector participation will be encouraged in respect of:
(a) access of private river transport operators to the port
of Kinshasa;
(b) development of private container stuffing - unstuffing
facilities in Matadi outside the Port area.
(c) Certain maintenance and stevedoring functions.
(d) developing a container handling and management company, or a
well-defined semi-private subsidiary, to take charge of
container operations at Matadi and Kinshasa terminals and
inland (para. 6 above).
(ii)     CFMK:
9.       The operation on CFMK is a simple point-to-point movement starting
from Kinshasa and ending at Matadi with loaded trains, and in the reverse
direction with empty trains or imported products. The time-tabled running
time is about 11 hours, while the actual time taken currently is over 14
hours. Apart from some improvement possible here through better
coordination of operations, it is necessary to eliminate detentions to
wagons in Kinshasa and Matadi railway yards for loading, unloading, train
examination and documentation. The turn around time for wagons is
currently 17 days which is unacceptable. The transit time for copper from
arrival at Kinshasa till discharge at Matadi is currently 10.3 days, and
this could be reduced to 7 days. This will call for reliable unjt trains



-70-
with air-braked, roller-bearing fitted wagons dedicated for transporting
export copper, and for proper coordination at the intermodal point at
Kinshasa. With the reduction in turn around time, the requirement of wagon
stock would also decrease drastically. The loads of train on the section
would be 700 gross trailing tonnes with Krupp GE locomotives and 850 tons
with the heavier Alsthom locomotives on order. Double-headed operation with
air-braked stock with 1400T/1700T gross trailing loads would assist in
reducing the utilizatica of section capacity and also reduce the
expenditure on train ranning staff. With the unloading/loading/handling
time not being a critical constrai.tc with containerization, double-headed
operation would be the logical method of operation, particularly in the
background of the investment in modernizing the braking system to air
brakes.
(iii)    River Transport Operations:
10.      Measures would include:
(a) Formation of integral and homogeneous convoys with suitable
tugs.
(b) Organisation of convoy movement on a planned link system to
ensure matching of loaded/empty convoy availability with
arrival of tugs.
(c) Proper seasonal operations planning to adjust tug allocations
between the Zaire and the Kasai to suit river conditions.
(d) Concentration of services on the primary lines (the Zaire and
Kasa.; rivers), with the secondary services being gradually
left to private operators.
(iv)     SNCZ:
11.      The importance of running copper export trains as integral point-
to-point trains becomes apparent when the figure of intermediate yard
detentions are seen:
Average detention
Yard                                 to goods trains (Hrs.)
Lubumbashi                                  29
Likasi                                      85
Tenke                                       18
Kamina                                      84
Mwene-Ditu                                  36
Kananga                                     29
Total                                      281
With a proper ob of intensive train examination done at the
originating point, and Llowing for safety re-examination at these
intermediate yards, the target detention at each of these yards should not
exceed 4 hours. The excess taken at these yards is, therefore, 257 hours
or 10.7 days. This is directly reflected in the transit time of copper.
In the turn-around of wagons, it reflects an excess of 21.4 days over the
target on account of yard detentions alone. The savings in locomotive and
wagon time would release considerable fleet capacity available for tapping



-71-
repressed transport demand. They would also translate in considerable
savings of foreign exchange outlays for renting rolling stock at the inter-
exchange with southern railways, mainly SAR costing US$7 million/year.
(c) Monitoring of Performance:
12.      Operational performance indicators have been evolved for each of
the interactive entities (Attachment 1). Each entity will review its
internal performance at quarterly intervals and prepare Progress Reports
showing an appropriate breakdown of its performance compared to the
targeted indicators. Reports will be sent to tutelle departments and to
the Association.
13.      Interfacial relationships and operations will also be
quantitatively defined, and accountability for performance would be
strictly enforced. This would include levying of demurrage and wharfage
charges, and penalties for contract defaults.
14.      Salient operational performance indicators will be included in the
"Contrat de Pragramme'su between ONATRA and SNCZ on the one '.and, and the
Government on the other, so that achievement of the specifiej targets
becomes a contractual obligation.
(d) Personnel Discipline and Performance:
15.      In parallel with the implementation of the project component to
develop human resources and strengthen personnel management, a concerted
drive to improve discipline of personnel will be instituted by the
management of each entity. This would lay emphasis on attendance,
timekeeping, product quality, production norms, as well as professional
honesty and integrity. Managements will tighten up routine and surprise
inspection procedures to monitor the success of this drive.
B. Performance Indicators
16.      Detailed performance indicators targets have been established
based on the project implementation schedule and the type of measures to be
taken (Attachment 1) the base year is 1987 and these targets years have
been selected for 1990, 1992 and 1995. Special attention was given to
productivity rates by employee.
17.      Projected Transit Times on the Voie Nationale are detailed in
Table 2.01. The present average transit times of 4Z.54 days (ex-refinery
to Matadi) are expected to be reduced to 30.18 days in 1990 and 20.72 days
in 1995. This performance would increase the through put capacity of the
Voie Nationale by over 50% while staffing is expected to decrease by about
20 to 25%.
18.      Operational Action Plan Bar Charts.  The time based action plans
of ONATRA and SNCZ are shown in Attachments 2 and 3 respectively and
reflect the detailed documents submitted by ONATRA in April 1988 and SNCZ
in July 1988.
19.      A joint letter from the PDGs of ONATRA and SNCZ sent to the Bank
on August 24, 1988 has confirmed the intent to carry out the Operational
Action Plan and enclosed a sumnary of the two detailed Operational Action
Plans in the forma- of a detailed matrix of measures to be taken
(Attachments 4, 5 and 6).

negociations entre la Republique du Zaire et la Banque Mondiale du 8
au 20 Juillet 1988, nous avons 1'honneur de vous transmettre en annexe
le Plan d'Action Operationnel finalis6 y compris son calendrier d'exe-
cution.
En ce qui concerne l'interface a Ilebo, la S.N.C.Z. et
1'ONATRA 4tabliront. le document commun d6finissant les responsabilites
aux points des interfaces et les modalites de fonctionnement, les res-
ponsabilites pour retards ainsi que les surestaries afferentes qui
vous sera communique avant le 31 Decembre 1988.
Nous vous prions d'agreer, Monsieur le Diiecteur, l'assurance
de notre consideration distinguee.

ANNEX 3
ZAIRE
FIRST TRANSPORT REHABILITATION PROJECT (ONATRAISNCZ)
Human Resources Development
I. ONATRA
A. ONATRA's Personnel Situation
1.       Staff.  In December 1986 ONATRA had 18,395 permanent and 589
temporary employees. The workforce declined by about 1,500 (7.5%) between
1982 and 1986, che period reviewed during appraisal of the project (Table
2.02). However, this development occurred at the expense of the technical
and to the benefit of the administrative functions and without any analysis
of the real needs of each service or unit. Thus, administrative staff
departements grew over the period by 375 (10%) while production staff LA1
by 1,448 (9%). Moreover, some units have been depleted of qualified staff
because retirees have never been replaced.
2.       Notable also is the grade distribution of the staff: 80 senior
managers (0.5%); 1,601 middle-level managers (8.7%); 4,932 foremen (26.8%)
and 11,782 workers (64%). Managerial staffing is particularly high in the
administrative departments or services (40%) and abnormally low in the
technical departments (for example, 3% in Ports). This low proportion may
help to explain the maintenance and production problems at ONATRA. In
contrast, the foremen/workers ratio is excessively high, averaging 41.5%.
In some services or workshops the foremen/workers structure is almost
line4r.
3.       In 1987 ONATRA still did not have an appropriate promotion policy.
This was illustrated by the fact that, during the period reviewed, of 539
management posts to be filled, 412 were filled by internal promotion and
127 by external recruitment. This had a negative impact on the production
apparatus because rather than recruit engineers, ONATRA systematically
promoted long-service foremen to managerial positions without giving them
suitable training and evaluating them adequately. On the other hand, the
number of foremen has risen sharply (49%). In most cases this increase is
due to artificial overgrading to help the employees concerned obtain a
higher salary. This situation has created extremely difficult probiems in
operating the job grading system, maintaining occupational qualifications
norms and organizing production.
4.       Education and Training Background. Of 602 managers only 192 have a
higher or university education. The number of engineers is excessively low:
there were 54 out of 1,181 supervisors ar.d managers (in December 1986). The
professional level of foremen and workers is also critically low. The Human
Resources Directorate (Direction des Ressources Humaines - DHR) has
performed a systematic evaluation of 3,000 employees based on both
theoretical and practical psychotechnical and occupational tests. Only
1,800 employees (60%) have qualifications consistent with the posts they
occupy, while the other 1,200 show no interest in supplementary training by
reason of their age and their very low level of education. For example, in
shipyards, 67% of foremen have no education at all and barely 62 have upper
secondary level or higher level.



- 88 -
5.       Retirement and Staff Reduction.  The legal retirement age is 61,
with early retirement possible from age 55. The possible pension after 20
years of service is equal to 75% of full pension. Employees with 73 years
of service or more are entitled to full pension. All retirees receive an
installation allowance varying from 6 to 12 months' salary and geared to
length of service. The transition from a true salary with all allowances to
a pension represents a very sharp fall, of 70 to 80 percent of salary.
6.       Employees rarely retire before the age of 61. The Zairean
Government nas recently accepted a proposal from ONATRA, urder which
employees aged 55 years with 30 years of service will automatically be
retired and will receive sustantial retirement compensation (up to 6
months' salary). This would have enabled the retirement of 785 employees
at the end of 1987, at a compensation cost of the order of Z 106 million.
In this way, ONATRA could reduce its staff to a reasonable figure over a
few years, which optimum would have to be determined through a systematic
analysis of each ONATRA function and a thorough study of the new
organization chart, to be implemented under the proposed project.
Retirements would allow ONATRA to shed unproductive employees and at the
same time recruit young employees who are better educated, more dynamic and
more receptive to technical innovation. ONATRA would have to adequately
identify its true needs and set up a recruitment plan; however, given the
current capability of ONATRA, it is still doutbful that this work could be
achieved correctly without external assistance and special supervision.
B. ONATRA's Management Tools
7.       It follows from the above that ONATRA's current personnel
management system is inadequate to achieve its main goals, which are to
keep control of employee numbers and to ensure that the occupational
qualification norms for each category and each trade are respected. First
of all, while employee numbers have fallen during the past period, this
development has occured at the whim of internal pressures and at the
expense of the production sector. Secondly, appointments and promotions
have completely distorted the occupational stratification and this has
severely impaired the personrucl structure and the organization of the
worshops and technical units.
8.       Yet ONATRA possesses management tools that could be effective: the
manpower planning chart and the ONATRA organization chart. Until January
1985 the manpower planning chart served to guide personnel movements,
particularly recruitment. It was drawn up by the DHR and adjusted in an
annual meeting, where the PDG, DHR and department directors defined the
staffing structure for each year, by units, levels and Job categories. In
principle, every recruitment request, regardless of level, had to pass
through the Personnel Department, which checked that it was consistent with
the manpower planning chart projections. At the present time, the manpower
planning chart is becoming more a compendium of statistics - incidentally,
incomplete - and above all no longer serves to control staff movements.
9.       In principle, ONATRA's organization chart should be regarded as a
management tool for determining the staff authorized for the coming year.
In fact, there is no budget by workforce and the Finance Department
performs no control of personnel management actions that have a financial
impact. In addition, the current organization chart is not of much use



- 89 -
because it provided for 20,032 employees whereas actual staff totalled
18,300 in December 1986; this demonstrates once again the shortcomings in
establishing the personnel needs of each department.
C. Restructuring of the ONATRA's DRH
10.      ONATRA's shortcomings in manpower forecasting and control. of
occupational qualification norms are due to both structural and human
causes. Both functions are entrusted to the Human Resources Directorate
(DHR), which has two subdirectorates: Ergology (Ergologie), and Training
Coordinatton. The Ergology Subdirectorate is divided into a Qualifications
Service and a Selection Service. The Training Coordination Subdirectorate
comprises the Training centers and the Technical Studies Unit. In addition
to manpower forecasting and qualification control, DHR is responsible for
organizing recruitment tests. The Selection Service appears to be
functioning well; the Qualifications Service is still embryonic. Training
coordination has been in a critical situation since the position of
Subdirector became vacant in April 1986. The ORT adviser, financed under
the ongoing project, has been compelled to provide substitution assistance
to keep training going. Besides this, some other weaknesses have been seen:
(i) the Director'entire time is taken up organizing the recruitment
examinations, (ii) its staff have no adequate qualifications except for the
Selection Service, and (iii) they do not have the requisite experience or
profile in either manpower planning or training.
D.  Future of Personnel Administration at ONATRA
11.      The following is an outline of recommended future policies/actions
for better personnel administration:
(i)      The Selection and Recruitment function should be separated from
DHR. This detachment would be to relieve the DHR of the burden
of external recruitment activities and allow it to perform the
remaining functions undisturbed.
(ii)      There is an urgent need to strengthen the job grading and
analysis, and the training units. To this end, it would be
advisable to: (a) recruit a statistician and at least two
analysts for the manpower planning, a mechanical engineer and a
electrical engineer for training, (b) study, with ORT, DHR's
job descriptions and redeploy the personnel on the basis of job
profiles and employee qualifications. Top priority should be
given to appoint a high-level and competent staff member to
head the DHR. The person would need to be: (a) very
knowledgeable about ONATRA's human resources, (b) experienced
in manpower planning, (c) receptive to new personnel
management techniques, particularly job grading and analysis,
and (d) familiar if possible with the various in-house training
strategies and policies.
(iii)     ONATRA should urgently rehabilitate the personnel functions.
The proposed work program comprises several components:



- 90 -
(a) preparation of a personnel master plan, which will have to
take into account new strategic approaches to be defined
soon, the real needs of ONATRA's various services, and
retirements over the next five years;
(b) a job classification study, which should be carried out
with the aid of technical assistance and the participation
of the employees'representatives;
(c) training of personnel managers in the new personnel
management techniques;
(d) study of the salary structure and the current system of
salery supplements, and proposal of a new compensation
system, with simulations of the financial impact of each
alternative and its effect on compensation in each
occupational -ategory;
(e) setting-up of a Human Resources Committee whose functions
would be to define CNATRA's human resources development
policy and strategy, and rule upon the annual staffing
plans proposed by the departments;
(f) study of workforce reduction aiming at (i) a second
contingent of employees aged 55 and with 30 years of
service in December 1988, and (ii) the contingents that
will meet this criterion later in 1989, 1990 and 1991;
measures should also be studied for encouraging retirement
by employees regarded as surplus and unproductive and for
defining the financial impact of each alternative to be
proposed;
(g) study of management procedures for decentralizing
recruitment function while respecting the qualifications
norms; this work should be done with the aid of technical
assistance, either zairian or expatriate. It is noted that
appointments and promotions up to Chief of Service (Al)
have been decided on at the department level; on the other
hand, all recruitments, regardless of level, have to be
approved by the Director General's Office. It is therefore
necessary tot (i) draw up management, recruitment and
promotion procedures, (ii) leave to the departments
managers the responsibility and initiative to recruit
employees in accordance with the annual staffing plan
finalized by ONATRA's Human Resources Committee, and (iii)
institute a system of staffing controls and monitoring of
personnel movements.
E. Trainint at ONATRA - Situation in 1987
12.      Training at ONATRA has been provided at the following centers: (i)
shipyard training center, (ii) navigation training center, (iii) railway
training center, (iv) Matadi Port training center, (v) Administration
training center and, (vi) Police training center. In the past the training
centers were placed under direct control of the relevant Departments, i.e.



- 91 -
the Shipyard Training Center was attached to the Shipyard Department, the
Navigation Training Center to the Navigation Department etc... According to
ORT'study, this organization was inefficient and training performance was
far from being satisfactory for the following reasons: (i) low rate of
equipment use for some centers while the same equipment was needed in
others, (ii) duplication of the same training activities in different
centers, (iii) disparity in the qualifications of teaching staff, (iv) no
control of training quality, and (v) no standardization of training.
13.      In 1984, ONATRA's management decided to apply ORT's
recommendations, by attaching all of its training centers to the
Directorate of Human Resources (DHR), which received from ORT an important
program of technical assistance of about 200 man-months. Since then, many
actions have been performed: (i) regrouping the same training sections in
center, (ii) evaluation of teaching staff's qualifications, (iii) transfer
of underqualified to production - more than one-third of permanent teaching
staff, (iv) intensive ini-service training for trainers. These actions seem
to have benefitted the training centers as the total number of training's
man-months climbed from 2,325 in 1984 to 6,786 in 1986. Regarding the
training of the work-force in the production sector, two important
realizations must be mentioned: the evaluation and analysis of 3,000 work
posts covering 23 different trades, (ii) the development of a training
methodology appropriate to the needs assessed in the wake of the workpost
analysis. This methodology was used with good results in three areas of the
Shipyard at Ndolo (machine shop, diesel engines and sheet metal).
productivity was on the rise following the reorganization measures
undertaken bv ORT specialists. However the sustainability of new systems,
procedures and work habits established by the ORT spe^.ialists were
seriously questionee due to the overall deterioration of the working
conditions at the Shipyard.
14.      The contribution of ORT has been significant on the technical
side, but there has been no progress at all on the management side at the
Directorate of Human Resources. Instead, the general situation has been
seriously deteriorating since the transfer of the Director and the Training
Coordinator to new posts in 1986, which appears to have resulted in
reduced interest for manpower management and training, and a reduction in
ORT specialists' influence.
F. Future of Trainint at ONATRA
15.      The following are recommendations for the future of training at
ONATRA.
(i)     There is a strong need for a close coordination between
training and production at ONATRA: training activities
should not start without a thorough analysis of the
production apparatus and an evaluation of the staff,
taking into account the requirements of various jobs
defined in terms of tasks, skills and/or attitude. The
last evaluation operation carried out in 1985-86 with ORT
assistance proved very useful by revealing the fact that
40% of the ONATRA staff were unqualified to perform their
respective job.



- 92 -
(ii)    The capability of the training unit should be reinforced.
Staff in ONATRA have not yet been made aware of the new
concept in training , let alone how to implement it.
ONATRA has no choice but to urgently reinforce the DHR
(para. 12 ) and personnel management units at Department
level.  This would call for adequate technical assistance
to help DER in the following tasks: (i) analysis of the
remaining sobs which have yet to be defined, (b)
evaluation of staff on the basis of the official
organization chart, (c) design of an appropriate
retraining program for each group of trades and at each
level.
(iii)   There is also a need to set up a Human Resource
Development Committee chaired by the President Managing
Director, whose mandate consists of: (i) formulating
general policy for training and retraining and examining
key strategies and policies for personnel administration
and management; (ii) adopting new rules and procedures
related to the assignment and hiring of staff to make
management more clear-cut and less arbitrary.
G. ONATRA's 1988 - 1992 Training Plan
16.      The 1988-92 training plan was developed by ONATRA with the
assistance of ORT and revised by a Bank specialist in last November. This
followed several studies on ONATRA's main functions and activities and its
prospects for future development and restaffing. The general objective of
the training plan is to: (i) improve the qualifications of staff who can
benefit from further training as identified through the staff evaluation
operations conducted by the ORT consultants; (ii) train new staff to
recruit in accordance with ONATRA's development plan for the concerned
period and, (iii) improve the management and organization capability of
managers and supervisors.
17.      The training plan also has some more specific objectives which are
defined as follows:
(i)     train staff of Port and Railway Departments in effective
use and proper maintenance of the new and sophisticated
equipement already in place and/or to be imported during
the implementation of the new Project;
(ii)    reinforce the function of maintenance in all technical
units. This training will call for an active participation
from unit managers in: (a) elaborating procedures of
mainte.iance which have not existed so far; (b' training
the staff in accordance with the new procedures and
periodically evaluating them with the assistance of the
training unit;



- 93 -
(iii)   reinforce the function of procurement which will be
restructured on the basis of the new concept, organization
and working procedures. The new system to be introduced
with the assistance of a consulting firm will also require
recruiting new staff and therefore coherent training
activities.
(iv)    help implement the new policy of decentralization and
reorganization of ONATRA'structures by training staff at
all levels in new procedures of manageruent.
18.      The training plan involves about 8,000 staff members in several
specialties (general mechanic, electricity, electronics, sheet metal, air
conditioning, welding, pneumatic, navigation...). In awdition, it
includes: (a) the training of 600 managers and foremen in management and
organization, (ii) the overseas training of about 100 staff members in the
specific techniques of management and operation for whom local training
would be difficult and costly.
19.      Training in basic specialties will be provided by ONATRA's
Training Centers to workers and foremen. Training in navigation will
continue to be given at ENAVI, training in general mechanics,
radiocommunication and railway technics at Railway Training Center, and
training in ship repair and welding at ETO and Chanic. All these centers
will receive $US 600,000 worth of training equipment financed by the on-
going (Credit 1180-ZR) and proposed Projects. Training in management will
be conducted by local training institutions such as CEPETEDE and CENACOF
with the assistance of training specialists called for under the proposed
project. Emphasis will be placed on special training seminars for each
group of about 15 supervisors or managers. The Tra&ning Unit and
Department Training Committees will work closely with consultants in
prepariag programs and etablishing requirements for lecturers. The
overseas training would consist of organizing training courses designed
either for fields of speciliazation for which it would be difficult to
organize seminars in Zaire, or for top-level managerial staff whose
knowledge needs to be updated periodically in order to keep up with
developments in ONATRA's operations and organization.
20.      As to in-service training, the new methodology of training
integrated to production proposed by ORT will be developed in all workshops
to obtain a better efficiency of training and a direct impact on
production. Technical assistants provided by the different Technical
Assistance Programs should actively participate in implementing this
methodology by giving advice in shop organization, production planning, job
analysis, and in-service training. On the other hand, the training
activities proposed by OFERMAT will continue with closer supervision by the
Railway Training Center and the Training Unit. The evaluation of these
activities should be carried out by managers of technical and training
units, recorded by the Personnel Management Unit and taken into account for
promotion and/or transfer.

II. SNCZ
A. SNCZ's Staffing Situation
21.      SNCZ staff was 22,004 by October 1987, not counting 1,800 hired ad
hoc and paid on daily basis. Of the permanent staff, 48% (10,672) were
unskilled, 40% (8705) semi-skilled or skilled, 9% (1967) middle managemeant
and 3% (660) management. Main features of the staffing have been notedt (i)
the total number of staff, that fluctuated from 20,500 to 23,000, has not
much changed over the last ten years; (ii) the ratio of management is very
low (3%), and particularly criticLl in the Technical Department (2,4Z) snd
in the Operation Department (1,2Z); (iii) more than a thousand of skilled
workers are holding positions of middle management, due to lack of staff in
this category; and (iv) the number of 99 highly trained engineers (5 years
of training in School of Engineering or University) for all SNCZ
Departments seems also low; for example, in the Operation Department, out
of more than 6,000 staff, there are only 5 engineers.
22.      Age of staff. The average age of SNCZ management staff (middle or
high) is still good. Most of them are in the bracket 30-45 (68%); those in
the bracket 45-60 represent only 22% and could be replaced with no major
difficulty, when they retire. The real problem is how to ensure more
adequate recruitment and training plans to strenghten management staff
according to SNCZ's real needs, but this could not be done without
reorganizing and restaffing the Personnel Directorate. As to workers, the
average age is relatively high; those whose age is over 45 represent 442.
Since most of them work under severe climatic and physical conditions in
maintenance of SNCZ installations, the productivity is very low. With a
view to compensating for this deficiency, technical department chiefs
prefer to recruit external workers on a temporary basis, who are younger
and more motivated. In fact the departure of those in the worker category
is even desirable, since this will enable SNCZ to replace poorly trained
workers with low productivity with young people who are better educated and
more adaptable to technological change. According to labor legislation in
Zaire, however, employees who reach the age of 61 are entitled to retire
but not cannot be forced to retire without their consent. There is really
little hope for SNCZ to solve this crucial problem independently of the
Governments's policy in employment.
23.      Salary structure. SNCZ staff are, in general, by far more well
paid than employees in the governmental sector. In order to keep pace with
inflation in the past ten years, SNCZ mangement has had to dramatically
increase the contribution in the form of allowances and benefits paid and
provided. For workers, basic salary in average represents only 30% of total
labor costs, while allowances and benefits account for 70X. Due to this
disproportion, if the ratio between the basic salary of non-skilled workers
and that of highly qualified workers is adequate and equivalent to 3.2. On
the contrary, the ratio between the total remunerations - including basic
salary, allowances and benefits- paid to the same non-skilled and highly
qualified workers has abnormally reduced to 1.6. On the other hand, the
remuneration of a highly skilled worker is t Lree times less than that a low
management staff ranked immediately upper, and promotion from the worker
category to the low management position is rather exceptional. The adverse
consequences of this situation are obvious. In several cases there are
reports of a decline in motivation, an increase in absenteeism, and a
relaxation in discipline. It is important that the salary structure along
with the job classification would be revised entirely during the proposed



- 96 -
project, with a view to improving workezs'motivation and ensuring their
career development, and at the same time increasing SNCZ's produc.;ivity.
24.      Personnel management. The Personnel Department is composed of 5
divisions and services which are responsible for: administration of higher
level staff, administration of low level staff, organization of recruitment
tests, inspection, and collection of personnel data. While the structure is
capable of dealing with daily administrative work, it is not adequate to
carry out the main functions of manpower planning and staff qualifications
control. According to a CEGOS study, the pay organization that relies on an
outdated data processing system is also heavy and unreliable, and should be
revised entirely. Relations between the Personnel Department and other
central or ragional entities have yet to be defined in terms of procedures
governing recruitment, appointment, transfer, promotion, and sanctions and
the delegation of autho.-ity at each level of responsibility. As to staff,
while most of them have a higher education degree, they have not been
trained in new techniques of personnel management.
24.      Manpower planning. SNCZ has no permanent structure responsible for
manpower planning. This constitutes a main failure of the personnel
management system. Since 1976, about 5,000 staff have retired, more than
7,000 have left the corporation for reasons of dismissal, resignation and
desertion, and 11,000 new agents have been recruited. The only management
tool that SNCZ possesses to deal with this complex situation is the
organization charts, which are unjustifiably set up independently, one for
management staff and o.ae for workers. The organization charts define the
hierarchial rank of each post within a service and the required
professional qualifications. In practice, they are used as reference
whenever the Personnel Department is searching for candidates to fill
vacant positions in the category of management staff. But, they are useless
for manpower planning, as data are incomplete, unreliable and not
systematically updated. Technical assistance would be required to help SNCZ
put in place a manpower plamning system and train personnel managers as
well as production unit chiefs in implementing it; the proposed process
would also call for active participation from production unit chiefs in
forecasting recruitment needs and staff movements, and in evaluating
performances of their services and staff, with a view to periodically
adjusting the initial manpower plans.
26.      Job classification. SNCZ staff is composed of several job groups
and categories: civil engineeringt signals and telecommunications,
mechanical and electrical engineering, marine, architects and surveyors,
accountants, personnel, training and education, medical care, supplies,
traffic, data processing, secretaries, and artisans. The job classification
prepared some fifteen years ago is far from fitting to SNCZ's complex
employment situation and responding to the main objectives of a state
transport enterprise. Several -eaknesses have been identified: (i) jobs
posts are graded depending on academic achievements rather than job
specifications and required professional qualifications or experience; for
example, most of locomotive drivers or marine off'cers are likely treated
as skilled workers, in spite of their great responsibility and professional
experience; (ii) administrative jobs are more paid than technical or
production jobs; this is visible with track maintenance staff who, in
general, are graded below clerical staff, although the first oclupational
group requires a training longer than the second one; (iii) the job
classification system does not permit to regroup job titles into a
manageable number of job groups and categories, and thus to ensure staff



- 97 -
development career; (iv) the passage from the category of skilled workers
to the category of low management staff is quite restrictive, in spite of
the fact that more than a thousarnd of skilled workers are holding positions
of m&nagement. These problems, along with inadequate remuneration and a
perceived absence of career prospects, have hurt staff motivation.
Technical assistance would be needed to help set up a new system of job
classification. The main purpose of such assistance would be to help to
prepare the methodology used to classify SNCZ jobs and to train the
national analysts responsible for continuing this work.
27.      With regard to personnel management, SNCZ should take steps to
accomplish the following: (i) establish job descriptions for staff together
with the required professional qualifications and experience; (ii) complete
a detailed inventory of management staff and workers; (iii) restructure the
Department if Personnel and create two new units - manpower planning and
professional qualification standard; (iv) complete the formulation of
mechanisms, procedures and rules for personnel management and introduce
these at the production unit level; (v) train personnel management staff
and initiate them in the manpower process, (vi) revise the job
classification system and salary stru-cture including *urrent salary
supplements, (vii) establish a new pay system that would ensure easy and
reliable calculation and control, and (viii) implement a process for
progressively decentralizing personnel management, after retraining and
evaluating personnel management staff at regional units.
B. Trainint Situation in 1987
28.      Organization. Training at SNCZ is administered at the central
level by the Training Division, which comprises two units (services)s one
responsible for training of managerial staff, the other being in charge of
training of workers. The Training Division is attached to the
Administrative Directorate. Coordination between training activities and
personnel management is rather weak. At the organizational level, with a
view to ensuring a linkage between training and the production sectors,
some major moves were made by the former Management, viz: (i) the
establishment of the Manpower Committee chaired by .he SNCZ General Manager
ana responsible for formulating a general policy in training and for
adopting the annual plan and budget for training activities, (ii) the
creation of department-.l training conmmittees, which are responsible for
assisting the Training Division in identifying training needs, designing
training programs, evaluating training performances and providing teaching
staff and technical support as needed. In fact, the Manpower Committee is
-.ot quite active due to many changes at the Management level - 4 General
Managers from 1984 to 1988. On the contrary, Department training committees
are meeting from time to time.
29.      Training of workers. This training is provided in 4 centers:
Likasi Training Center, Commercial and Traffic Training Cente-, Permanent
Installations Training Center, and Kalemie Marine Training Cente'. In 1987,
1,300 workers were trained totalising 4,i91 man-months of training.
Distribution by workers and sectors is as follows: 466 in transport, 205 in
traffic control, 28 in personnel administration, 187 in permanent
structures, 278 in equipment, and 137 in signalisation and
telecommunication. In general, there are 4 levels of training, each one
prepares trainees to a definite citegory of the job classificatimn system.
In some specialties, there is however a fifth level which prepares to the
lowest step of the supervisory category. The duration of training is from 2



- 98 -
to 8 months depending on degrees and specialties. Besides these, there is a
4 year program for technicians in industrial electricity and machine shop.
30.      The training of workers mainlv aims at preparing potential workers
to have adequate qualifications for an eventual promotion. Those who are
successful in one level of training must return to work for at least 2
years- in practice more than 3 years; then they could return to the next
level of training, if they have very good job rating and performed well in
the last training and if the further training is Droposed by their service
with adequate justifications. This is an excellent approach aiming at: (i)
integrating training into the process of staff career development, (ii)
improving training efficiency by combining theory and practice. There
remains however much to be done to obtain expected results. Training
programs must be revised periodically by professional committees, with a
view to adapting them to rapid technological development and ensuring a
coherent liaison among different levels of training. Follcv-up on training
should also be organized more systematically jointly by the concerned
Departments and Training Division. The selection of trainees should be done
in a fair way to offer to workers the same opportunity for career
development and not only to the most capable workers. To this end,
selection criteria should be set up by the Training Division and applied
unevently to all Departments. Finally, special attention should be paid to
developing a program the objective of which is to train potential workers
to have required qualifications before being promoted to the low level
management category; the selection could be done easily among a thousand of
qualified workers who are now occupying positions in this category. As to
training facilities, they vary from one center to another. The Likasi
Training Center created in 1932 is adequately equipped; its architecture
has been designed for training; besides this, it has many possibilities for
future extension. On the contrary, the other centers are former office
buildings transformed into classrooms and worshops. A study would be
conducted during implementation of the proposed project with a view to
examining whether it wotald be more efficient and economic t- transfer the
two Lubumbashi Training Centers to Likasi and to create a unique center for
all specialties by providing additional classrooms and worshops.
31.      Training of managerial and supervisory personnel. This training
started in 1983 without any external assistance. Since then, good progress
have been seen. In 1984, the training was limited to young University
graduates, and aimed at making them acquainted with SNCZ's organization
during the probationary period before hiring them permanently. In 1987,
training activities were provided to some 360 staff from administrative and
technical departments and the average length of training was about 3 weeks.
Programs are set up jointly by the Training Division and relevant
Departments; the latter assist also in detaching their oun qualified
specialists to reinforce the teaching staff.
32.      There is still much room for improving this very important
program. SNCZ has yet to define an overall policy in this matter, which
should be based on staff performance evaluations and an in-depth analysis
of weaknesses in organization and production. This could not be done
however without technical assistance and a clear definition of the SNCZ's
restructuration and development program for the next coming years. As
mentioned earlier (para. 28 ), little attention is paid by the new
management to manpower and training problems, and the Training Division has
not been involved at any phase in preparing the next development plan. This
failure should be remedied as soon as possible. There is also a lack of



- 99 -
facilities: training for high level staff has to be organized in the two
Lubumbashi training centers, which have yet to deal with their own problems
of accommodation. The construction plan for a new center has been proposed
by SNCZ, but there is a need for a feasibility study to analyze more in
details future training activities, with special consideration to the
following: number and professional category of traineees, specialties,
localization, coordination with other existing centers, inve3tment costs
for construction and equipment.
33.      Teaching staff. In general, SNCZ's teaching staff has an average
service of 6 years and at least 3 years of teaching experience. During the
implementation of the on-going project, 12 staff of the Division of
Training have been given a complementary overseas training in teaching
methods and new technics of their own specialization. Moreover, every 2 or
3 years, trainers must return to jobsites for at least 3 months with a view
to getting them acquainted with new equipment and production methods. In
spite of significant efforts in building up a strong teaching staff, there
has still been a difficult problem of career development. Since they are
highly qualified specialists, they could not get regular promotion with the
Training Division unless they reintegrate into their former technical
department where they have better chance to hold higher position. Measures
should be taken to: (i) assign a special status to permanent trainers, with
the possibility of career development, and (ii) require SNCZ' best staff to
participate in training as temporary trainers as part of their
responsibility.
C. Training Plan for 1989-92
34.      The training plan for 1989-92 was developed by SNCZ's staff
without any external techniZal assistance. The plan comprises two parts;
the first part represents the continuation of the present plan, which has
the following main objectives: vi) train annually about 250 new staff
members. (ii) upgrade selected workers by providing them additional
competence that could lead to an eventual promotion, and (iii) develop a
better match between the quali"ications of staff and jobs. The second part
of training has more specific objectives relevant to the main institutional
reforms initiateo  by the proposed project; they are to: (i) help implement
the new management system to be introduced by technical assistance and
properly keep them in operation before the corresponding technical
assistance programs are over, and (ii) train staff in effective use of the
nev equipment to be imported under the proposed project.
35.      The training plan involves about 3,234 workers totalising 16,707
man/months in several tradess general mechanic, electricity, electronic,
diesel, transport, commercial, traffic, sheet metal, welding, permanent
structures. Training in navigation will be conducted by the ONATRA
Navigation Training Center, in view of limited demand in this area. In
addition, the plan includes the training of about 842 managers and
supervisors in management, organizat!.n and various technical fields.
Emphasis will be placed on identified priority sectors: (i) new procedures
in the financial, personnel, commercial and data processing functions, (ii)
new techniques for planning, procurement and management control, and (iii)
new techniques of production and workshop organization.
36.      Several modes of training will be used to help implement the
training plan: (i) regular training at Lubumbashi and Likasi SNCZ's
training centers, (ii) in-plant training carried out by mobil* teams of



- 100 -
trainers composed of consultants and national staff, (iii) on-the-job
training to be handled by unit chiefs and consultants; and (iv) training
provided by specialized national centers such as CEPETEDE, CENACOf,
CFC/GECAMINES and INPP. Technical assistance should actively participate in
training. To this end, specific clauses on training should be integrated
in its terms of reference, and its contribution should be defined and
organized in close coordination with the Training Division. In addition,
the training plan includes overseas training for about 120 staff members
(30 per year) in specific techniques of management and operations, for
which local training would be difficult and costly.

ANNEX 4
ZAIR
FIRST TRANSPORT REHABILITATION PROJECT (ONATRAISNCZ)
Det-iled Project Description
ONATRA
Port of Matadi
1.       Rehabilitation of Piers 3 and 4 and log storage area.  This area
will be used for shipment of logs as well as for general cargo, including
mainly imports of fresh foods. The pier's pilings are well preserved but
the superstructure is in bad condition and needs to be reconstructed. The
works which would be carried out by contractors include electric, water,
and fire protection networks, demolition of rear-quay warehouses, 25000 m2
of log storage paved yards, and re-laying of railway tracks. Though the
rehabilitation of piers 1 and 2, upstream, would be deferred, minor repairs
and fendering are included in the project.
2.       New maintenance shop.  Maintenance operations of port equipment
are scattered over the entire port area, carried out itn ill-equipped and
poorly located and disorganized sheds. A new maintenance shop will be
located ir the downstream end of the port, beyond the area of possible
expansion. It will include shops for heavy engine repairs. An area at the
second level of the warehouses will be rehabilited for other maintenance
operations.
3.       Small craft dolphins.  About 15 barges, used for river traffic and
lighterage, are moored in unsafe and old facilities; three new breasting
dolphins will solve the problem.
4.       Completion of water distribution and fire protection works.  The
project provides potable water distribution for piers 8, 9 and 10 and
improvements to t-u- _,re protection system, including pumps, emergency
electrical -gpoly, and high pressure pumps on board the tugs.
5.       Relaying of railway tracks.  Continuing the rationalization
started under ongoing projects, and to accommodate the new surfaces, it
will be necessary to relas some 3000 m of tracks within the port.
6.       Rehabilitation of cranes.  ln May 1987, there were 54 rail mounted
cranes, of which 45 are quay-side and 9 rear-side, and of these 10 are out
of service and the rest are generally in very poor condition. The
electrical components of 31 of these cranes are currently being renewed
under A,DB financing. The availability of the cranes is very low due to
port maintenance problems, difficulties in getting obsolete parts, and to
their poor condition. On the other hand the containerization trend and the
cnanging nature of the traffic have reduced the need for conventional quay-
side cranes. The project includes the rehabilitation of the mechanical
components of the foilowing cranes:  (i) twelve 3/6 ton, (ii) two 5110 ton,
and (iii) three long reach 316 ton.
7.       Carzo handling equipment.  The existing cargo handling equipment
is old, poorly maintained, and insufficient for the most pessimistic



- 102 -
traffic projectAons. The proposed equipment (tractors, forklifts, tire
mounted container cranes, trailers, spreaders, wagon pushers) respond to a
1992/95 horizon and represents a minimal approach, on the assumption that
privately owned equipment will continue to operate in the port. A list is
included with the Cost Estimates. It was agreed that part of this list can
be substituted for full rehabilitation of used equipment due to its
training value even if the cost is expected to be the same.
8.       Maintenance and Shop Equipment.  There is urgent need in the port
for a mobile lubrication unit, as well as a maintenance truck in order to
facilitate the maintenance of the rail mounted cranes and other immobilized
equipment. In addition, the new maintenance shop will require machine
tools, welding equipment, and other repair/maintenance tools.
Port of Kinshasa
9.       Repairs to "Quai Kisangani".  The project provides for repairs to
the superstructure and pavement, and storm water collection trenches.
10.      Storm Drains.  Continuation of the current works which did not
include storm drains for the totality of the port area, mainly the new
"Quai sur pieux".
11.      New Maintenance Shop.  Maintenance operations are scattered over
the entire port area, carried out in ill-equipped and poorly located and
disorganized sheds. Contrary to Matadi, at Kinshasa it should be possible
for ONATRA to subcontract much repair work with private shops, but a small
facility will still be required for the rail mounted cranes and major
equipment. It will include shops for light and heavy engine repair shops,
welding, electric motors, plate work, and spare parts warehouse. It is
envisaged to use an existing structure for most of the shop needs.
12.      Rehabilitation of Cranes. There are currently 25 cranes in the
port, of which nine are ready to be scrapped and two are new; of the
remaining 14, nearly all in bad condition, the project will rehabilitate
the mechanical components of 8 (four 3/6 ton, two 2.5/5 ton, one 10 ton,
and one 20 ton) and the remaining cranes will either be re-located to ports
of the interior or scrapped.
13.      Cargo Handling Equipment.  Cargo handling equipment is old, in bad
condition and insufficitnt for the traffic projections. A minimal
equipment replacement program has been retained, including forklifts, log-
handling front-end-loader, mobile crane, trailers, spreaders, ani slings;
the list is included with the cost estimates and it was agreed to the
rehabilitation in lieu of new for part of the equipment.
River Navigation
14.      Cargo Barges.  Coherently with the future envisaged for ONATRA's
river operations - limited to the transport of minerals, logs, and certain
cargos along the main fluvial axis (River and Kasai) - the project includes
six C-500 cargo barges (this 500 ton capacity barge will continue to be the
standard for ONATRA operations). The total fleet of C-500 barges needs to
be increased to 54 from the current total of 48, in order to permit a more
efficient minerals transport by doing loading/unloading operations to a
convoy stationed at the terminus, without the line pusher, thus minimizing
the time the pusher remains at either end. The standardization will also



- 113 -
allow the assignment of more barges tu the Kasai during the low water
season, and to the River in the other season. The large pre-1950 barges
still in operation will be scrapped.
15.      Passenger Barges.  In order to restore Integrated Convoy services
to recent levels (one weekly arrival from Kisangani), and to satisfy
current demand, recognizing that this would serve primarily the traders
bringing foodstuffs to Kinshasa, the project includes six new C-50G type
passenger barges. The pushers incorporating deluxe and first class cabins
would be phased out, permitting the use of a standard pusher both in the
River and Kasai operations. This would allow increased use of pushers in
the Kasai during the low water season and to Kisangani during the Kasai
high water season.
16.      Pushers.  In order to satisfy the demand for addliional convoys in
the Voie Nationale during the low water season, and to increase "Trading
Convoys" on the River, the project includes two additional Pushers type LU-
950 CV. In addition a smaller 450 CV unit is included for restoring
"Trading Convoys" on the Kasai.
Shipyards
17.      Civil Works.  The physical plant of the N'Dolo shipyard has
suffered from neglect, abuse and lack of maintenance. While a study is
carried out to decide how to reorganize or disvest its activities, many
relatively minor repairs are urgently required, including: (i) replacement
of broken piles and sheetpiles on the piers; (ii) paving or resurfacing
storage areas; (iii) monorails at some shops; (iv) construction of a sand
blasting shed; 'v) foundations for new equipment; and (vi) compressed air
network.
18.      Equipment.  Relatively modest investments are required to replace
and modernize some of N'Dolo's obsolete equipment, notably: (i)
rehabilitation of cranes; (ii) rehabilitation of the boat displacement
carriages; (iii) a tractor and a trailer; (iv) additional welding machines;
(v) machine tools; and (vi) pumps. While these investments are absolutely
necessary for improving the productivity of the shipyard, they are
certainly not sufficient to guarantee increased productivity, and will have
to be accompanied by major institutional changes.
CFMK Railway
19.      Civil Works.  Relatively minor works are required at Mbanza-Ngungu
and Limete locomotive maintenance facilities, consisting mainly of
upgrading existing installations, adding some berths, and additional crane
structures. In order to improve security, the project provides lighting at
selected shops and stations.
20.      Wagon Modernization.  The project provides materials for the
conversion of 350 wagons to roller bearings, as well as miscellaneous shop
equipment.
21.      Locomotive Maintenance.  In order to improve locomotive
maintenance, badly needed shop equipment will be provided for the Mbanza-
Ngungu and Limete shops, as well as an important lot of spare parts.
22.      Track Maintenance and Miscellaneo.xs equipment.  The project
includes a track recording car, forklifts, *ehicles, and mobile cranes.



- 104 -
Containerization of the VN
23.      The project aims at increasing the containerization of export
products (mainly copper) as a means to decrease the travel time in the V.N.
thus improving the transport system efficiency. A lump sum has been
included to cover the acquisition of containers and to set up the needed
organizational structures (existing physical facilities are probably
sufficient). The subject will be considered as part of a broader study
covering container operations, domestic and international (para. 3, Annex
1).
Information Resources
24.      The project provides for the development of an Information
Resources Master Plan, including Software and Hardware coherent with the
future organization structure and revised role of ONATRA.
25.      Telephone System.  The project provides for the design,
procurement and installation of a modern telephone system for external and
internal communication between the various departments of ONATRA.
Human Resources
26.      ONATRA's project component would include the rehabilitation of the
personnel function. This would be done by: (i) preparation of a personnel
master plan; and (ii) two studies of (a) job classification and salary
structure, and (b) management procedures for decentralizing recruitment of
the Departments. Technical Assistance would be provided for implementing
the management procedures reforms and workforce reduction included in the
rehabilitation of the personnel function.
27.      The project also includes ONATRA's 1988-1992 training plan,
developed by the agency with help of consultants ORT (Switzerland) which
has been reviewed by and is acceptable to IDA. Broad objectives of the
plan ares (i) to improve the qualifications of staff who can benefit from
further training as identified through the consultants' staff evaluation;
(ii) train new staff to be recruited; and (iii) improve management and
organization capabilities of managers and supervisors. Specific objectives
of the plan are: (i) train staf. 'rom Ports and Railways Departments in
effective use and maintenance of equipment to be procured under the
project; (ii) improve overall maintenance through new procedures and
training on them; (iii) reinforce procurement function; and (iv) help
implement the decentralization policy by management staff training.
28.      The training plan involves (i) 6,000 staff at lower levels; (ii)
1,000 middle-level managers and foremen; and (iii) 100 senior managers. In
total, this is about 50% of staff.   Other staff either need no training or
would not benefit from it because of age or level of qualification.
Training will be provided at ONATRA's Training Centers to workers and
foremen, by local management training institutions and in some cases
through overseas courses.

(i)    Technical Assistance to personnel management for the

The track rehabilitation would include rehabilitation of the infrastructure
(formation, drainage and waterways) over the lengths involved. Other
sections of the track have also been surveyed, and'urgently needed
infrastructural works will also be carried out. Some 25,000 tons of rails,
220,000 metal sleepers, 260,000 concrete sleepers, 120 sets of points and
crossings and associated fastenings would be procured by SONATRAD or behalf
of SNCZ, and the track renewal works will be carried out partly by
contractors (266 kms) and partly by force account (37 kms). Supervision of
works will be done by consultants.
38.      Locomotives and wagons.  An important locomotive renewal program
is provided for in the project. Seven main line electric locomotives and
six diesel shunting locomotives will be acquired to replace locomotives
being scrapped. Spare parts will be provided for 70 heavy and intermediate
overhauls to locomotives, as well as one year's requirements of spares for
preventive maintenance of the overhauled fleet. The project also provides
for the rehabilitation and overhaul of 1200 wagons.
39.      Track maintenance machinerv and spares.  Provision is made in the
project for the acquisition of three track tampers, three ballast
regulators, one ballast grader, two raillroad cranes, as well as tools and
equipment for replacement and modernization. One set of mobile quarry
equipment is envisaged to augment ballast production capacity, and to ease
logistics of ballast movement.

competence, and (iii) develop a better match between qualifications of
staff and jobs, in particular help to implement the new system of
management to be introduced by the project and train staff in effective use
of the new equipment.
44.      The training plan involves about 3,234 workers totalling 16, 700
man-months in basic trades, and 800 managers and supervisors in management,
